Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Why Selling a Free Person with a Slave Invalidates the Entire Contract
Explanation of why combining free and slave in one sale is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two reasons the Hanafi school gives for the invalidity of a sale combining a free person and a slave, and explain why the analogy to marriage (nikah) is rejected.

Core Idea
Stipulating the free person's acceptance for the seller's profit amounts to riba, and splitting the contract at inception is invalid.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced case from the Hidayah: selling a free person together with a slave in a single contract. The Hanafi school rules this sale invalid due to a fasid condition that generates unjust profit (riba) and because it constitutes a partial sale at inception (bay' bi al-hissah ibtida'an). We also explore the intra-madhhab dispute with the Sahibayn, who analogized to marriage, and see why that analogy fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its basis
Understand that the sale of a free person and a slave in one contract is ruled as fasid (invalid) by Abu Hanifa. The text gives two reasons: the fasid condition (stipulating the free person's acceptance) and the partial sale at inception.
The term 'fasid' here indicates the contract is voidable and must be rescinded.
2
Analyze the fasid condition and riba argument
The seller gains profit (500 rupees) without any countervalue from the free person, which is riba. This makes the condition fasid, and a fasid condition in a sale renders the entire contract fasid.
Contrast with marriage, where fasid conditions do not invalidate the contract.
3
Examine the partial sale at inception (bay' bi al-hissah ibtida'an)
By including a free person (non-commodity) with a slave, the sale effectively allocates part of the price to the free person without proper specification, which is impermissible at the time of contract.
Hanafi distinguishes between ibtida'an (invalid) and baqa'an (valid) for such sales.
4
Evaluate the Sahibayn's qiyas to nikah and the Hanafi response
The Sahibayn argued by analogy to marriage: since fasid conditions do not invalidate nikah, the sale should also be valid. The Hanafi response is that there is a clear difference (farq) between bay' and nikah, making qiyas invalid.
This shows the importance of identifying the 'illa and the scope of analogy in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيوع جواب پہلے اعتراض کا جواب یہ ہے کہ آزاد اور غلام دونوں کا شمن علیحدہ علیحدہ بیان کرنے کے باوجود صفقہ ایک ہے کیونکہ نہ تو لفظ بیع مکر رہے اور نہ لفظ شراء مکر رہے اور جب تک لفظ بعث یا اشتریت میں تکرار نہیں ہو گا صفقہ متفرق نہ ہوگا۔ دوسرے اعتراض کا جواب یہ ہے کہ آزاد آدمی کے اندر بیع قبول کرنے کی شرط میں بائع کا نفع ہے اس طور پر مثلاً آزاد اور غلام کو ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض فروخت کیا اور آزاد مال نہیں ہے اور جب آزاد آدمی مال نہیں تو اس کے مقابلہ میں شمن بھی نہیں ہوگا پس یہ ایسا ہو گیا گویا بائع نے کہا کہ میں نے اس غلام کو پانچ سو روپیہ کے عوض اس شرط پر فروخت کیا کہ مشتری اس کے علاوہ پانچ سور و پید اور دے۔ پس بائع نے ایسے پانچ سو روپیہ کا نفع اٹھایا جو عوض سے خالی ہیں اور یہی ربوا ہے پس آزاد کے اندر بیج کو قبول کرنے کی شرط بشرط فاسد ہوئی اور شرط فاسد سے چونکہ بیع فاسد ہو جاتی ہے اس لئے غلام کی بیج بھی فاسد ہو گی دوسری بات یہ ہے کہ آزاد اور غلام کو ایک صفقہ کے تحت فروخت کرنے میں بیع بالحصہ ابتداء نا جائز ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں غلام اور آزاد دونوں کی بیع نا جائز ہوگی۔ بخلاف النکاح سے صاحبین کے قیاس کا جواب ہے جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ بیچ کو نکاح پر قیاس کرنا درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ نکاح شروط فاسدہ سے فاسد نہیں ہوتا البتہ بیع شروط فاسدہ سے فاسد ہو جاتی ہے پس فارق کے ساتھ قیاس کس طرح درست ہو سکتا ہے
The book of sales. Answer to the first objection: It is that even though the price (thaman) of the free person and the slave is mentioned separately, the contract (safqa) is one, because the word of sale (bay') is not repeated nor is the word of purchase (shira'). As long as the words ba'tu or ishtaraytu are not repeated, the contract is not divided. Answer to the second objection: It is that in the condition of the free person accepting the sale, there is profit for the seller. For example, the free person and the slave were sold for one thousand rupees, and the free person has no wealth. When the free person has no wealth, there will be no price in exchange for him. So this becomes as if the seller said: I sold this slave for five hundred rupees on the condition that the buyer give an additional five hundred [for the free person]. Thus the seller gained five hundred rupees without countervalue, and that is riba. So the condition of the free person accepting the sale became a fasid condition, and since a fasid condition invalidates the sale, the sale of the slave also becomes fasid. The second point: In selling a free person and a slave in one contract, sale by portion (bay' bi al-hissah) at inception is impermissible, so in this case the sale of both the slave and the free person is invalid. Answer to the qiyas of the Sahibayn [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] from marriage: The summary is that comparing sale to marriage is not correct because marriage is not invalidated by fasid conditions, while sale is invalidated by fasid conditions. So how can qiyas be valid when there is a difference?
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from the Hidayah commentary. It addresses the issue of selling a free person together with a slave in a single contract. The author, Ashraf Ali Thanwi, explains two objections and their answers. First, even if separate prices are mentioned, the contract is still one unless the words are repeated. Second, the condition that the buyer must accept the free person's ability to contract gives the seller profit without consideration, which is riba, making the condition fasid and the sale fasid. Additionally, this constitutes a partial sale at inception (bay' bi al-hissah ibtida'an), which is impermissible. The author then refutes the analogy to nikah made by the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), clarifying that nikah is not invalidated by fasid conditions, unlike sale. Therefore, the qiyas is invalid, and the Hanafi position stands: the sale is invalid.

Key Terms
صفقہ (safqah): The contract of sale as a single transaction. The text says the safqa is one even if multiple items are sold, as long as the words are not repeated.
شرط فاسد (shart fasid): A condition that is invalid according to Shari'ah, which renders the contract fasid (voidable) in the case of sale.
ربا (riba): Usury or unjustified increase; here, the seller getting 500 rupees without corresponding countervalue from the free person is considered riba.
بیع بالحصہ (bay' bi al-hissah): Sale of a portion of the commodity without specifying the portion at the time of contract. In Hanafi, this is invalid if done at inception (ibtida'an) but valid if it arises after contract (baqa'an).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, why is selling a free person and a slave together in one contract invalid?
A) Because the contract is divided into two separate sales
B) Because it involves a fasid condition that gives the seller profit without countervalue (riba) and constitutes partial sale at inception
C) Because a free person cannot be the subject of a sale contract at all
D) Because the Sahibayn ruled it invalid

Key Takeaway
Selling a free person with a slave in one contract is invalid due to the fasid condition causing riba and the impermissible partial sale at inception.

The Validity of Selling Mudabbar, Mukatab, Umm Walad, and 'Abd Ghayr
A ruling with proof using reasoning (talil) and analogy (qiyas).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the sale of mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, and 'abd ghayr is valid but suspended (mawquf), and distinguish it from the invalid sale of a slave with a free person.

Core Idea
Mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, and 'abd ghayr have monetary value, so their sale is valid but suspended; the conditions that corrupt a sale do not apply when they are sold with a regular slave.

This passage from Hidayah tackles a subtle issue: when a slave is sold together with a mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, or a slave belonging to another, is the sale valid? The author establishes that these categories are valid subjects of sale because they possess both servitude and property value. Their sale is suspended pending permission, but that does not prevent the regular slave's sale from being valid. This ruling is contrasted with the earlier case of selling a slave with a free person, which is batil.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
List the four types of slaves mentioned: mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, and 'abd ghayr. Note that each has a special status but still retains servitude (riqqiyyah) and property value (taqawwum).
The common enemy is that they are not fully free; they have some right to freedom but are still slaves.
2
Understand why they have monetary value
The passage states that a slave's value derives from both servitude and being considered property. Since these four have both, their monetary value (maliyah) is established, making them valid subjects of sale.
This reasoning (talil) is the basis for the permissibility of selling them.
3
Recognize the suspension (mawquf)
Although the sale is valid in substance, it is suspended upon permission: for mudabbar and umm walad, the qadi's judgment; for mukatab, his own consent; for 'abd ghayr, the owner's permission. Without permission, the sale can be rejected.
The rejection of the sale after refusal is proof that the sale existed initially.
4
Apply to selling a regular slave together with one of them
When a regular slave is sold together with, say, a mudabbar, the sale of the regular slave is valid because no fasid condition arises: the mudabbar himself is a valid subject, so there is no need to stipulate acceptance of a non-sale item, nor does partial sale at inception occur.
This contrasts with selling a slave with a free person, where the free person has no monetary value.
5
Compare with the invalid scenario
The passage explicitly contrasts: selling a slave with a free person is batil because the free person is not a valid subject, leading to a fasid condition and partial sale at inception. Here, none of those defects exist.
This comparison helps solidify the rationale.

Source Text

اور مدبر، مکاتب، ام ولد اور عبد غیر کے اندر رقیت بھی موجود ہے اور متقوم ہونا بھی موجود ہے پس جب دونوں باتیں موجود ہیں تو ان کی مالیت بھی قائم ہے اور جب مالیت قائم ہے تو ان کا محل بیع ہونا بھی ثابت ہے یعنی مالک کی اجازت پر موقوف ہے یہی وجہ ہے کہ عبد غیر کی بیچ غیر یعنی مالک کی اجازت ہے نافذ ہوتی ہے اور مکاتب کی بیج اس کی رضا مندی سے نافذ ہو جاتی ہے اور مدبر کی بیع قاضی کے حکم سے نافذ ہو جاتی ہے اور مدبر ، مکاتب اور ام ولد نے خود اپنی ذات کے مستحق ہونے کی وجہ سے بیع کو رد کر دیا اس لئے ان کے اندر بیع رد ہو گئی ہے اور بیع کا رد کرنا اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ ان کے اندربیع موجود تھی اور جب مدبر وغیرہ میں بیع متحقق ہوگئی تو مدبر، مکاتب، ام ولد اور عبد غیر بھی مبیع ہوئے اور جو غلام ان کے ساتھ ملا کر فروخت کیا گیا وہ بھی طبع ہے۔ تو ان صورتوں میں مبیع کے اندر بیع قبول کرنے کیلئے غیر مبیع کے اندر بیع قبول کرنے کی شرط لگا نا لازم نہ آیا اور جب یہ شرط لگانا لازم نہیں آیا تو غلام کی بیچ بھی فاسد نہ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ مفسد بیع یہی شرط تھی ۔ اور بیع بالحصہ ابتداء بھی لازم نہیں آیا کیونکہ مد بر وغیرہ جب بیچ کے تحت داخل ہو گئے تو ابتدائے عقد میں غلام اور مدبر وغیرہ پر من تقسیم کرنے کی نوبت بھی نہیں آئے گی بلکہ یہ نوبت بیچ کے بعد قبضہ کے وقت آئے گی اور اس کو بیع بالحصہ بقائے کہا جاتا ہے اور بیع بالحصہ بقاء جائز ہے اسلئے ان صورتوں میں غلام کی بیع درست ہو جائے گی۔ اسی وجہ سے غلام اور مدبر میں سے ہر ایک کا شمن بیان کرنا شرط نہیں ہے اور یہ ایسا ہے جیسے کسی نے دو غلام فروخت کیلئے اور مشتری کے قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے ایک ہلاک ہو گیا تو دوسرے غلام کی بیع اس کے حصہ ثمن کے عوض جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ اس میں نہ تو غیر مبیع کو قبول کرنے کی شرط لگانا لازم آتا ہے اور نہ بیع بالحصہ ابتداء لازم آتا ہے حالانکہ یہ دونوں باتیں ہی بیع کو فاسد کرنے والی ہیں۔ صل یہ کہ غلام اور آزاد کو ملا کر فروخت کرنے کی صورت میں چونکہ مبیع کے اندر بیع قبول کرنے کیلئے غیر مبیع کے اندر بیع قبول نے کی شہہ لگا نا لا زم آتا ہے اور بیع بالحصہ ابتداء لازم آتا ہے۔ اسلئے اس صورت میں غلام اور آزاد دونوں کے اندر بیع باطل ہے۔ اور نا ۔ اور عد ہر کی بیچ میں چونکہ یہ دونوں خرابیاں لازم نہیں آتیں اسلئے اس صورت میں غلام کی بیع جائز ہے۔
Regarding the second issue: Mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, and 'abd ghayr enter under the contract of sale because their monetary value (maliyah) is established. Their monetary value is established because a slave's value is realized through servitude (riqqiyyah) and being considered property (taqawwum). In mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, and 'abd ghayr, both servitude and being considered property exist. So when both are present, their monetary value is confirmed, and when monetary value is confirmed, they are valid subjects of sale. However, their sale is suspended (mawquf): the sale of a mudabbar is suspended upon the qadi's judgment; the sale of a mukatab is suspended upon his own consent; the sale of an umm walad is also suspended upon the qadi's judgment according to the two Shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad); and the sale of 'abd ghayr is suspended upon the permission of its owner (the other). This is why the sale of 'abd ghayr becomes effective with the owner's permission, the sale of mukatab becomes effective with his consent, the sale of mudabbar becomes effective with the qadi's order, and similarly the sale of umm walad becomes effective with the qadi's order. In any case, the sale of mudabbar etc. is suspended. However, since in the case of 'abd ghayr the other (owner) rejected the sale due to his entitlement to his slave (the subject), and mudabbar, mukatab, and umm walad rejected the sale due to their entitlement to their own selves, the sale was rejected in their cases. The rejection of the sale is proof that the sale existed in them. And when the sale was realized in mudabbar etc., then mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, and 'abd ghayr became sold items, and the slave that was sold together with them is also naturally [included]. So in these cases, it was not necessary to stipulate in the sold item the condition of accepting the sale for the non-sold item. And when it is not necessary to stipulate this condition, the sale of the slave will not be fasid, because that condition was the corrupting factor. Also, partial sale at inception (bay' bi al-hissah ibtida'an) is not necessary because when mudabbar etc. enter under the contract, at the inception of the contract there is no need for division between the slave and mudabbar etc.; rather, this need arises at the time of possession after the contract, and this is called partial sale after contract (bay' bi al-hissah baqa'an), which is permissible. Therefore, in these cases the sale of the slave is valid. For this reason, it is not a condition to state the price of each of the slave and the mudabbar separately. And this is like someone selling two slaves, and before the buyer takes possession, one dies; the sale of the other slave is valid in exchange for its share of the price, because in that case neither does the condition of accepting the non-sold item become necessary, nor does partial sale at inception become necessary, while both these things corrupt the sale. Summary: In the case of selling a slave and a free person together, because it becomes necessary to stipulate the condition of accepting the sale for the non-sold item in the sold item and partial sale at inception becomes necessary, therefore the sale of both the slave and the free person is batil. And in the case of the slave and mudabbar etc., because these two defects do not become necessary, therefore the sale of the slave is valid.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from the Hidayah commentary. It addresses a second issue: the validity of selling a slave together with a mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, or 'abd ghayr (a slave belonging to another). The author explains that these categories of slaves have monetary value because they possess both servitude and property status, so they can be sold. However, their sale is suspended (mawquf) because it depends on external permission: for mudabbar and umm walad, the qadi's judgment; for mukatab, his own consent; for 'abd ghayr, the owner's permission. Importantly, when sold together with a regular slave, the issues of a fasid condition or partial sale at inception do not arise because the mudabbar etc. are themselves valid subjects of sale. Therefore, the sale of the regular slave is valid. The author contrasts this with the earlier case of selling a free person with a slave, where the free person is not a valid subject, leading to invalidity. He also provides an analogy: if two slaves are sold and one dies before delivery, the sale of the remaining one is valid, as no fasid condition or partial inception is involved.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death through a declaration of tadbir.
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitabah contract to purchase his freedom in installments.
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, giving her a special status upon the master's death.
عبد غیر ('abd ghayr): A slave belonging to someone else, not the seller. The seller is not the owner.
بیع موقوف (bay' mawquf): A sale that is valid in substance but depends on external approval to become effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of a regular slave together with a mudabbar valid, according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the mudabbar has no right to freedom.
B) Because the mudabbar has monetary value and is a valid subject of sale.
C) Because the sale of mudabbar is already complete without conditions.
D) Because the buyer must accept the mudabbar's freedom.

Key Takeaway
The sale of mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, and 'abd ghayr is sahih mawquf, and when sold with a regular slave, the slave's sale is valid.

Reconciling Batil vs Mawquf in Sale of Mudabbar, Umm Walad, Mukatab
Resolution of an apparent contradiction in Hidayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school classifies the sale of umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab as mawquf initially and batil after refusal, and apply this timing distinction to resolve apparent contradictions in fiqh texts.

Core Idea
A sale is mawquf before permission, batil after refusal — no contradiction, only different stages.

In the Hidayah, a student might be puzzled: one passage says the sale of umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab is batil (void), while another says it is mawquf (suspended). This lesson unpacks the reconciliation: the two statements refer to different moments in time. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi rulings on sales by limited-ownership slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two apparently contradictory statements
The matn says 'batil'; the sharh says 'mawquf'. Note the difference in legal categories.
2
Understand the categories batil and mawquf
Batil is void from inception with no legal effect; mawquf is valid in substance but suspended pending approval.
3
Recognize the timing distinction
The sale is initially mawquf; it becomes batil only if permission is denied or qadi does not approve.
4
Apply the reconciliation
No contradiction: the matn describes the eventual outcome after refusal, while the sharh describes the initial state before any decision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سوال... صاحب ہدایہ کی عبارت پر ایک اشکال ہے وہ یہ کہ اول باب میں متن کی عبارت ہے و بيع ام الولد والمدير والمكاتب باطل ، اور یہاں صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ ان کی بیع موقوف ہے۔ جواب ... اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ان کی بیچ اس وقت باطل ہے جبکہ مکاتب اجازت نہ دے اور مد بر اورام ولد کی بیچ کے جواز کا قاضی قسم نہ کرے لیکن اس سے پہلے ان کی بیع موقوف ہے۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب جمیل احمد عفی عنہ
Question: There is an objection on the wording of the author of Hidayah: In the beginning of the chapter, the text of the matn states: 'The sale of umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab is batil.' But here the author of Hidayah says that their sale is mawquf. Answer: The answer is that their sale is batil when the mukatab does not give permission and the qadi does not rule on the validity of the sale of the mudabbar and umm walad. But before that, their sale is mawquf. And Allah knows best the correct. Jamil Ahmad 'afi 'anhu.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This is a critical refinement from the commentary. A student raises an apparent contradiction: the matn of the Hidayah says the sale of umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab is batil (void), but the sharh (commentary) says it is mawquf (suspended). The author resolves this by explaining the timing: initially, before any permission or judgment, the sale is mawquf—meaning it has the potential to be valid but is not yet effective. It becomes batil only after the mukatab refuses permission or the qadi does not approve. Thus, there is no contradiction: the matn refers to the final outcome after refusal, while the sharh describes the initial state. This is a common reconciliation technique in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
باطل (batil): Void, invalid from its inception with no legal effect.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; a contract that is valid in substance but depends on external approval to be effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author of the commentary reconcile the apparent contradiction that the matn says sale of mudabbar is batil while the sharh says it is mawquf?
A) The matn was abrogated by later scholarship.
B) The sale is mawquf initially and becomes batil only after the qadi refuses to approve.
C) The matn refers to a different category of slaves.
D) The sharh is correcting an error in the matn.

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction in Hidayah is resolved by timing: sale of umm walad, mudabbar, mukatab is mawquf initially and becomes batil only after permission is denied or qadi does not approve.

Sale of Free Person and Slave Together is Void
Ruling on composite sale of free and slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a free person and a slave together and identify the two defects that make the sale bāṭil.

Core Idea
A sale combining a free person and a slave is bāṭil because it includes an impermissible condition and a sale by portion.

This lesson covers a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh regarding composite sales: when a free person and a slave are sold together in a single contract, the entire transaction becomes void. Understanding this rule prevents entering invalid contracts and highlights key principles about conditions and partial sales in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a single sale contract that includes both a free person and a slave as sold items. The Hanafi ruling is that the sale is bāṭil (void) for both.
Bāṭil means the contract has no legal effect from the start.
2
Identify the two defects
First, the contract implies a condition that the free person (who is not a valid subject of sale) must accept the sale, which is an impermissible stipulation. Second, it involves a sale by portion (bai' bil hissa) because the price is not allocated to each item individually.
These defects are inherent in the composite nature of the contract.
3
Contrast with separate sale
If the slave is sold alone, neither defect applies, so the sale is valid. The passage concludes that the separate sale of the slave is permissible.
The invalidity is specific to the combination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کی شرط لگانا لازم آتا ہے اور نہ بیع بالحصہ ابتداء لازم آتا ہے حالانکہ یہ دونوں باتیں ہی بیع کو فاسد کرنے والی ہیں۔ صل یہ کہ غلام اور آزاد کو ملا کر فروخت کرنے کی صورت میں چونکہ مبیع کے اندر بیع قبول کرنے کیلئے غیر مبیع کے اندر بیع قبول نے کی شہہ لگا نا لا زم آتا ہے اور بیع بالحصہ ابتداء لازم آتا ہے۔ اسلئے اس صورت میں غلام اور آزاد دونوں کے اندر بیع باطل ہے۔ اور نا ۔ اور عد ہر کی بیچ میں چونکہ یہ دونوں خرابیاں لازم نہیں آتیں اسلئے اس صورت میں غلام کی بیع جائز ہے۔
The condition of setting a condition becomes necessary, and the sale by portion does not become necessary initially, although both these things invalidate the sale. The conclusion is that in the case of selling a slave and a free person together, because in accepting the sale for the sold item, it necessitates setting a condition for accepting the sale for what is not part of the sold item, and the sale by portion becomes necessary initially. Therefore, in this case, the sale of both the slave and the free person is invalid. As for the separate sale of a slave, because these two defects do not apply, in this case the sale of the slave is valid.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage states a ruling about a composite sale where a free person and a slave are sold together in one transaction. The Hanafi position is that such a sale is bāṭil (void) because it contains two fatal defects: first, it implies a condition that is not part of the contract (shart lazim), and second, it involves a partial sale initially (bai' bil hissa) which is impermissible. However, if each is sold separately, the sale of the slave is valid. This is a straightforward ruling statement without proof.

Key Terms
بیع باطل (bai‘ bāṭil): A sale that is legally void from its inception due to a fundamental defect in the contract.
بیع بالحصہ (bai‘ bil hiṣṣah): A sale of a portion of an undivided share, often problematic in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a free person and a slave are sold together in one contract, what is the ruling in the Hanafi school?
A) The sale is valid for both.
B) The sale is valid only for the slave.
C) The sale is invalid (bāṭil) for both.
D) The sale is valid only for the free person.

Key Takeaway
Combining a free person with a slave in one sale invalidates both because it introduces impermissible conditions and a sale by portion.

Sale of Mudabbar, Umm Walad, and Mukatab: Mawqūf or Bāṭil?
Resolves an apparent contradiction in Al-Hidayah regarding the ruling of such sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings of mawqūf and bāṭil for the sale of mudabbar, umm walad, and mukatab, and explain the condition that changes the ruling.

Core Idea
A sale of these slaves is mawqūf until permission is denied; only then is it bāṭil.

This passage clarifies an apparent contradiction in Al-Hidayah. While the matn declares the sale of a mudabbar, umm walad, and mukatab as bāṭil (void), the commentary elsewhere says it is mawqūf (suspended). The resolution lies in the timing: the sale is mawqūf initially, becoming bāṭil only after a definitive refusal of permission. This distinction is crucial for understanding the legal status of such sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradiction
Note that the matn says the sale of these slaves is bāṭil, but Al-Hidayah's commentary says it is mawqūf. Recognize this as an apparent inconsistency that requires resolution.
The contradiction arises from reading the two statements out of context.
2
Understand the resolution
The answer explains that the sale is bāṭil only after permission is denied. Before that, it is mawqūf. This reconciles the two statements by showing they refer to different stages.
Mawqūf means suspended pending ratification.
3
Identify the conditions for bāṭil
For a mukatab, the sale becomes bāṭil if he does not grant permission. For a mudabbar or umm walad, it becomes bāṭil if the qadi does not permit the sale.
The permission condition differs based on the slave's status.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a scenario, determine whether the sale is mawqūf or bāṭil by checking if permission has been denied.
If no denial has occurred, the sale remains mawqūf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سوال... صاحب ہدایہ کی عبارت پر ایک اشکال ہے وہ یہ کہ اول باب میں متن کی عبارت ہے و بيع ام الولد والمدير والمكاتب باطل ، اور یہاں صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ ان کی بیع موقوف ہے۔ جواب ... اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ان کی بیچ اس وقت باطل ہے جبکہ مکاتب اجازت نہ دے اور مد بر اورام ولد کی بیچ کے جواز کا قاضی قسم نہ کرے لیکن اس سے پہلے ان کی بیع موقوف ہے۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب جمیل احمد عفی عنہ
Question: There is an objection to the wording of the author of Al-Hidayah: in the beginning of the chapter the text says: 'And the sale of the umm walad, the mudabbar, and the mukatab is invalid.' But here the author of Al-Hidayah says that their sale is suspended. Answer: The answer is that their sale is invalid only when the mukatab does not grant permission, and the qadi does not permit the sale of the mudabbar and umm walad. But before that, their sale is suspended. And Allah knows best. Jamil Ahmad, may he be forgiven.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This is a question-and-answer piece addressing an apparent inconsistency in Al-Hidayah. The matn states that the sale of these three categories of slaves (umm walad, mudabbar, mukatab) is bāṭil (void). However, in another place, the commentator (Al-Hidayah) says it is mawqūf (suspended). The resolution is that the sale is bāṭil only after permission is explicitly denied or the qadi rules against it. Before that, it is mawqūf, meaning the sale is conditional upon subsequent approval. This is an important distinction in Hanafi fiqh: a suspended contract is not void ab initio but rather pending ratification.

Key Terms
بیع موقوفہ (bai‘ mawqūf): A sale that is suspended pending ratification by the relevant party (e.g., owner or guardian). It is not void but incomplete.
ام الولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thus gaining limited freedom upon his death.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whom the master has declared free upon his death, making the slave partially free.
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah to purchase his freedom by paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The sale of a mudabbar, umm walad, or mukatab is initially mawqūf and becomes bāṭil only if permission is denied.

Possession in Fasid Sale: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Establishes Hanafi ruling on possession in fasid sale and rebuts Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership via possession in a fasid sale and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Possession with permission transfers ownership in a fasid sale; the prohibition does not negate the essence.

When a sale is fasid (defective but not void), does the buyer become owner by taking possession? The Hanafi school says yes, while Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position, focusing on the role of possession in curing a weak contract.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the buyer becomes owner and must pay the market price (qimah). Note the conditions: both countervalues are property (mal) and possession occurs with the seller's command.
The text says: 'مالک بن جائے گا اور قیمت لازم ہوگی'
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a prohibited act cannot confer ownership. The prohibition (nahy) negates the legality of the contract, so possession does not transfer ownership.
He analogizes to selling carrion or wine, which are void ab initio.
3
Follow the Hanafi rebuttal on the prohibition
The Hanafi response distinguishes between the essence of the contract (exchange of property) and the surrounding element that is prohibited. The prohibition does not nullify the essence; it only weakens it.
The text uses the example of sale during Friday call: the sale is valid but sinful.
4
Analyze the role of possession
Possession strengthens the weak cause, similar to a gift (hiba) which requires possession to be binding. Ownership is not immediate to avoid reinforcing the defect, but after possession it is established.
Before possession, the buyer cannot claim ownership; after, the defect becomes curable.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi says buyer owns the item and must pay the price; Shafi'i says buyer must return the item and no ownership transfers.
This affects liability if the item is destroyed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

عوض مال ہیں تو مشتری اس مبیع کا مالک ہو جائے گا اور مشترکی پر اس کی قیمت واجب ہوگی۔ اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ مشتری مالک نہیں ہوگا اگر چہ مبیع پر قبضہ کرے کیونکہ بیع فاسد ایک ممنوع چیز ہے تو اسکے ذریعہ ملک کی نعمت حاصل نہ ہوگی اور اس لئے کہ نبی مشروع ہونے کا نسخہ ہے کیونکہ دونوں متضاد ہیں اس وجہ سے قبضہ سے پہلے مفید ملک نہیں ہے اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ بائع نے مردار کے عوض بیچا ہو یا شراب کو دراہم کے عوض بیچا ہو ۔ اور ہماری میل یہ ہے کہ بیچ کار کن اس کے اہل سے صادر ہوا درانحالیکہ اپنے محل کی طرف منسوب ہے تو بیع منعقد ہونے کا قائل ہونا واجب ہوا اور اہل ہونے اور محل ہونے میں کچھ خفا نہیں ہے اور بیچ کارکن مبادلۃ المال بالمال ہے اور ہمارا کلام ایسی ہی بیع فاسد میں ہے۔ اور ہمارے ن ایک نہی مشروعیت کو مستحکم کر دیتی ہے کیونکہ ” نہی فعل منہی عنہ کے متصور ہونے کا تقاضہ کرتی ہے پس نفس بیع مشروع ہے اور اس نفس بیچ سے نعمت ملک حاصل ہوتی ہے اور ممنوع صرف وہ امر ہے جو اس سے لگا ہوا ہے جیسا کہ اذان جمعہ کے وقت بیع کرنا اور قبضہت پہنا، ملک اس لئے ثابت نہیں ہو گی تاکہ لگے ہوئے فساد کو مستحکم کرنے کا سبب نہ بنے اس لئے کہ مشتری سے مبیع واپس لیکر فساد بیع کا دور کرنا اجب ہے تو مشتری کا مبیع سپرد کرنے کے مطالبہ سے رک کر فساد دور کرنا بدرجہ اولی واجب ہوگا اور اس لئے کہ سبب فتیح کے ساتھ لگ بانے کی وجہ سے ضعیف ہو گیا پس مفید ملک ہونے میں اس کو قبضہ کے ساتھ قومی کرنے کی شرط لگائی گئی جیسا کہ ہبہ میں ہے اور مردار مال نہیں ہے اس لئے رکن معدوم ہو گیا اور اگر شراب مبیع ہو تو ہم (اول باب بیع الفاسد میں ) اس کی تخریج کر چکے ہیں اور دوسری بات یہ کہ
Section on its rulings: It says the rulings of fasid sale. If the buyer takes possession of the sold item by the seller's command in a fasid sale, and in the contract both countervalues are property, the buyer will become the owner of the sold item and the price becomes obligatory. Imam al-Shafi'i's view: He said the buyer does not become owner even if he takes possession, because it is prohibited, so the blessing of ownership cannot be obtained through it, and because the prohibition negates legality due to contradiction, hence it does not confer ownership before possession, and it becomes like when one sells carrion or sells wine for dirhams. Our evidence: The pillar of sale issued from its people (qualified parties) and was directed to its place (valid subject), so it is necessary to consider it effective. There is no concealment in the qualification or the validity of the subject. Its pillar is the exchange of property for property, and our speech is in such a fasid sale. The prohibition (nahy) establishes legality according to us because it necessitates the conceptualization of the prohibited act, so the essence of the sale is legal, and through this essence the blessing of ownership is obtained. The prohibited is only what is adjacent to it, like the sale at the time of the Friday call. Ownership is not established before possession to avoid reinforcing the adjacent defect, because it is obligatory to remove the defect by restitution, so it is even more obligatory to refrain from demanding (the property). And because the cause has become weak due to its association with something ugly, it is conditioned that it be strengthened by possession in conferring the ruling, like in a gift. Carrion is not property, so the pillar is absent. If wine is the subject, we have already derived its ruling elsewhere. And another thing is that in wine, the obligatory is the price, and it is suitable as a price not as a priced item.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute about the effect of possession in a fasid (defective but not void) sale. The Hanafi position: If the buyer takes possession of the item with the seller's permission, and both countervalues (price and item) are property (mal), the buyer becomes owner and must pay the market price (qimah). Imam al-Shafi'i holds that ownership cannot transfer because the sale is prohibited; the prohibition negates any benefit of ownership. The Hanafi rebuttal: The prohibition is on a surrounding element, not the essence of the exchange. The essence (the exchange of property) is legal. The prohibition only makes the contract weak, but possession cures that weakness. Ownership is not immediate to avoid strengthening the defect, but after possession, it is established. The analogy to carrion and wine fails because carrion is not property (so the pillar of sale is missing), and wine's ruling is derived separately.

Key Terms
بیع فاسد (bai‘ fāsid): A sale that is defective due to a missing condition or prohibited element, but not void ab initio; it can be rectified.
قبض (qabḍ): Physical possession or taking control of the sold item with the seller's permission.
قیمت (qīmah): Market value or price, payable by the buyer in a fasid sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale, when does the buyer become the owner of the sold item according to the Hanafi school?
A) Immediately upon concluding the contract.
B) Only after the seller's permission is obtained.
C) After taking possession with the seller's command.
D) Never; the sale is void.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, possession with permission transfers ownership in a fasid sale, and the buyer must pay the market price.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Ownership in a Defective Sale: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute on whether buyer owns the item in fāsid sale after possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ownership in a fāsid sale after possession and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A forbidden sale can still transfer ownership upon possession because the prohibition targets the context, not the essence.

This passage dives into a central dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: does a defective (fāsid) sale, where both countervalues are property, make the buyer the owner after he takes possession? The Hanafi school says yes, the Shafi'i says no. The reasoning hinges on whether the prohibition of the sale destroys its effectiveness or merely weakens it. Follow along to see how both schools construct their proofs.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: in a fāsid sale where both countervalues are property and the buyer takes possession with the seller's permission, the buyer becomes owner and owes the price (qīmah).
The text emphasizes 'qabḍ' (possession) and 'bi-amr al-bā'i' (with seller's command).
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds the buyer does not become owner even after possession, because the sale is prohibited and cannot confer the blessing of ownership.
The Shafi'i view is supported by Malik and Ahmad.
3
Follow the Shafi'i proof
Understand the two Shafi'i arguments: (1) a prohibited act cannot be a cause for a blessing (ownership), (2) the prohibition abrogates the lawfulness of the sale due to contradiction.
They compare the fāsid sale to selling carrion or wine for dirhams.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafi school rebuts: the prohibition does not abrogate lawfulness but rather confirms the essence of the sale as lawful; the prohibition attaches to an external factor (e.g., time/context). The cause is weakened, so possession is needed to perfect ownership.
Analogy to sale during Friday call: prohibited but not void.

صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے منع کرنے کی وجہ سے بیع فاسد کی مشروعیت منسوخ ہوگئی ہے اور بیع فاسد کی مشروعیت اس لئے منسوخ ہوگئی کہ نبی ( ممانعت ) اور مشروعیت کے درمیان تضاد اور منافات ہے کیونکہ نبی تقاضہ کرتی ہے فتح کا اور مشروعیت تقاضہ کرتی ہے حسن کا ۔ اور فتح اور حسن کے درمیان منافات ہے پس جب حسن اور فتح کے درمیان منافات ہے تو ان کے مقتضی یعنی نبی اور مشروعیت کے درمیان بھی منافات ہوگی اور جب ان دونوں کے درمیان منافات ہے تو یہ دونوں جمع نہیں ہو سکتے ہیں یعنی یہ نہیں ہو سکتا کہ ایک چیز مشروع بھی ہو اور ممنوع بھی ہو اور جب دونوں جمع نہیں ہو سکتے تو نبی وارد ہونے کے بعد مشروعیت منسوخ ہوگئی پس ثابت ہوا کہ بیچ فاسد کی مشروعیت منسوخ ہے یہی وجہ ہے کہ بیع فاسد قبضہ سے پہلے مفید ملک نہیں ہوتی۔ یعنی بیع فاسد اگر مشروع ہوئی تو قبضہ سے پہلے بھی مفید ملک ہوتی جیسا کہ بیع صحیح قبضہ سے پہلے مفید ملک ہوتی ہے پس قبضہ سے پہلے پیج فاسد کا مفید ملک نہ ہونا بھی بیع فاسد کی مشروعیت کے منسوخ ہونے کی دلیل ہے اور جس
Chapter on its rulings. It is about the rulings of a defective sale. The buyer took possession of the sold item in a defective sale by the seller's command, and in the contract both countervalues are property, so the buyer becomes the owner of the sold item and the price becomes obligatory. The viewpoint of Imam al-Shafi'i: He said: He does not own it even if he took possession, because it is prohibited, so he does not attain the blessing of ownership through it. And because the prohibition abrogates the lawfulness due to contradiction, and for this reason it does not benefit ownership before possession. And it becomes like when he sold carrion or sold wine for dirhams. Our proof: The pillar of sale was issued from its people (the seller) and attributed to its place (the item), so we must rule that it is concluded. There is no hiddenness in the capacity and the eligibility. Its pillar is the exchange of property for property, and the discussion is about this. And the prohibition establishes lawfulness according to us, because it necessitates the conceptualization [of the forbidden act]. Thus the essence of sale is lawful, and through this essence the blessing of ownership is attained. The prohibited thing is only what is adjacent to it, as in the sale during the call to prayer. Ownership is not established before possession lest it lead to affirming the adjacent corruption, because it is obligatory to remove the corruption by reclaiming [the item]. So refraining from demanding [return] is even more obligatory. And because the cause has become weak due to its association with something repugnant, it is conditioned for it to be strengthened by possession in giving the ruling, like the gift. Carrion is not property, so the pillar is non-existent. If wine is the price, we have already discussed that elsewhere. Another thing: In the case of wine, the obligatory is the value, and value is suitable as a price, not as a priced item. Translation: And if the buyer took possession of the sold item in a defective sale by the seller's command, and in the mentioned contract both countervalues are property, then the buyer becomes the owner of that sold item and its price becomes obligatory upon the buyer. And Imam al-Shafi'i said: The buyer does not become owner even if he took possession of the sold item, because the defective sale is a prohibited thing, so the blessing of ownership is not attained through it. And because the prohibition is an abrogation of lawfulness, as they are mutually contradictory. For this reason, it does not benefit ownership before possession. And this becomes like when the seller sold carrion in exchange or sold wine for dirhams. Our evidence: The pillar of sale was issued from its people while it is attributed to its place, so it becomes necessary to say that it is concluded. There is no hiddenness in the capacity and eligibility. The pillar of sale is the exchange of property for property, and our discussion is about such a defective sale. And according to us, the prohibition reinforces lawfulness, because the prohibition necessitates the conceptualization of the forbidden act. Thus the essence of sale is lawful, and through this essence the blessing of ownership is attained. The prohibited thing is only what is adjacent to it, like selling at the time of the Friday call. Ownership is not established before possession lest it become a cause for affirming the adjacent corruption, because it is obligatory to remove the corruption of the sale by taking back the sold item. So refraining from demanding the buyer to hand over the sold item is even more obligatory to remove the corruption. And because the cause became weak due to its association with something repugnant, it is conditioned for it to be strengthened by possession in giving the ruling, like the gift. Carrion is not property, so the pillar is non-existent. If wine is the sold item, we have already discussed its derivation earlier in the chapter on defective sale. The second matter: In the case of wine, only the value is obligatory, and value can be a price, not a priced item. Explanation: Ahkām is the plural of hukm, and hukm is called effect related to a thing. The effect related to a thing occurs after the thing. Therefore, first the thing, i.e., the defective sale, was mentioned, and after that its effect, i.e., the ruling, was mentioned. The ruling of sale is ownership. So in this chapter, there is a discussion on whether a defective sale benefits ownership or not. Note that according to us, within sale, fāsid and bāṭil are two separate things. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, both are the same; there is no difference between bāṭil and fāsid. The scenario: If the buyer took possession of the sold item in a defective sale with the seller's permission, and in the mentioned contract both countervalues are property, then the buyer becomes the owner of the sold item and its price becomes obligatory upon him. The word 'fāsid' is mentioned in the text because a bāṭil sale absolutely does not benefit ownership, neither before possession nor after possession. 'By the seller's command' means the seller's permission, whether explicit or implicit. If the seller explicitly permitted possession, then the buyer becomes owner by taking possession, whether he took possession in the seller's presence or absence. The implicit permission is when the buyer takes possession in the seller's presence in the contract session and the seller remains silent. If the buyer took possession in the seller's absence, he does not become owner because in that case neither explicit nor implicit permission is found. The condition that both countervalues be property is attached because if one or both countervalues are non-property, the sale is bāṭil, like selling a free person, selling blood, or selling carrion. In any case, according to us, the buyer's possession in a defective sale benefits ownership. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, possession in a defective sale does not benefit ownership. That is, even if the buyer took possession with the seller's permission in a defective sale, according to Imam al-Shafi'i the buyer would not become owner. Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad also hold this view. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof: The defective sale is a forbidden act, and obtaining ownership is a blessing. A forbidden act cannot be a cause for obtaining a blessing because appropriateness between cause and effect is necessary. There is no appropriateness between a forbidden act and the blessing of ownership; rather there is a kind of contradiction. Since there is no appropriateness between the forbidden act (i.e., defective sale) and the blessing of ownership, the blessing of ownership cannot be obtained through this forbidden act. Second proof: The practice of defective sale existed in the time of ignorance, but in Islam, defective sale was prohibited. So due to the prohibition of the Lawgiver (peace be upon him), the lawfulness of defective sale is abrogated. The lawfulness of defective sale was abrogated because there is contradiction and opposition between prohibition and lawfulness. Prohibition necessitates ugliness, and lawfulness necessitates goodness. There is opposition between ugliness and goodness. So when there is opposition between goodness and ugliness, there will also be opposition between their implications, i.e., prohibition and lawfulness. When these two are in opposition, they cannot be combined, meaning it cannot be that one thing is both lawful and prohibited. When they cannot be combined, after the prohibition comes, the lawfulness is abrogated. So it is established that the lawfulness of a defective sale is abrogated. This is why a defective sale does not benefit ownership before possession. That is, if a defective sale were lawful, it would benefit ownership before possession just as a valid sale benefits ownership before possession. So the fact that a defective sale does not benefit ownership before possession is also evidence that its lawfulness is abrogated. And this [reasoning]...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the ruling of ownership in a defective (fāsid) sale. The author first states the Hanafi position: if the buyer takes possession of the item with the seller's permission in a fāsid sale where both countervalues are property, the buyer becomes owner and must pay the price. Then he presents the view of Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that even with possession, the buyer does not become owner because the sale is prohibited and thus cannot confer the blessing of ownership. The Shafi'i argument includes two proofs: (1) a prohibited act cannot be a cause for a blessing, and (2) the prohibition abrogates the lawfulness of the sale. The Hanafi response, which follows, uses rational arguments (dalīl 'aqlī). The Hanafi position relies on the fact that the pillar of sale (exchange of property for property) is present and valid; the prohibition does not affect the essence of the sale but only the surrounding context. Just as selling during the Friday call is prohibited but the sale itself is not invalid, a fāsid sale's prohibition does not negate the ownership effect, though it weakens the cause, requiring possession to perfect ownership. The text also clarifies that this ruling applies only to fāsid (defective) sales, not bāṭil (void) sales, which are entirely ineffective. The passage guards against the misconception that any prohibition in a sale makes it completely invalid.

Key Terms
بیع فاسد (bay' fāsid): A sale that is valid in its pillars but has a defect in its conditions, e.g., ribā or ambiguity.
قبض (qabḍ): Taking physical possession or control of the sold item with the seller's permission.
ملک (milk): Ownership; the legal entitlement to derive benefit from a property.
قیمت (qīmah): The market value or price that the buyer must pay in a fāsid sale, not the agreed-upon price.
نعمت الملک (ni'mat al-milk): The blessing/benefit of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what condition is necessary for the buyer to become owner in a fāsid (defective) sale?
A) The buyer must pay the full price immediately.
B) The buyer must take possession of the item with the seller's permission.
C) The sale must be confirmed by a witness.
D) The seller must explicitly renounce the defect.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a fāsid sale transfers ownership upon possession because the prohibition weakens but does not destroy the cause of ownership.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Ownership in Fasid Sale: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i Dispute
Explains Hanafi ruling that fasid sale transfers ownership with possession, refuting Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ownership in a fasid sale and evaluate the Shafi'i proof against it.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, ownership transfers upon possession with permission, because the contract's essence is valid.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah addresses a key inter-madhhab dispute: whether a fasid (defective) sale can transfer ownership of the sold item to the buyer. The Hanafi school holds that fasid and batil are distinct, and that when the buyer takes possession with the seller's permission, ownership transfers and the buyer must pay the market price. The Shafi'i school, along with Malik and Ahmad, argues that a prohibited sale cannot yield the 'blessing' of ownership. We will dissect the arguments of both schools and understand the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the ruling: In a fasid sale where both countervalues are property, if the buyer takes possession with the seller's permission (explicit or implicit), the buyer becomes owner and must pay the market price.
Implicit permission is when the buyer takes possession in the seller's presence during the council of contract and the seller remains silent.
2
Identify the Opponent and Their View
Recognise that Imam al-Shafi'i (and Imams Malik and Ahmad) hold that even with permission, possession in a fasid sale does not transfer ownership.
For the Shafi'i school, fasid and batil are synonymous; both are completely invalid.
3
Follow the Opponent's Proof
Analyse the Shafi'i proofs: (1) No appropriateness between a prohibited act and the blessing of ownership. (2) The prohibition (nahy) abrogates the legitimacy of the sale. (3) Analogy with batil sales like carrion or wine where ownership never transfers.
The Shafi'i rely on the principle that a prohibited act cannot produce a beneficial legal result.
4
Analyse the Hanafi Response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: The pillar of sale (offer and acceptance) is valid; the prohibition only affects the corrupting condition, not the essence. Prohibition confirms legitimacy (nahy yugariru al-mashru'iyyah). The analogy with batil sales fails because in batil the pillar is missing.
Hanafi usul distinguishes between faskh li-ghayrihi (external nullification) and faskh li-dhatihi (essential nullification).
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: According to Hanafis, the buyer can validly resell the item after possession; according to Shafi'is, the seller retains ownership and can reclaim the item.
This difference affects who bears the risk of loss and rights of disposal.

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ہشتم
شراب کی صورت میں صرف قیمت واجب ہے اور قیمت ثمن ہو سکتی ہے بیچ نہیں ہو سکتی ۔ تشریح احکام حکم کی جمع ہے اور حکم اثر مرتب علی الشئی کو کہتے ہیں اور اثر مرتب علی الشئی چیز کے بعد ہوتا ہے اس لئے پہلے شئی یعنی بیع فاسد کو بیان کیا اور اسکے بعد اس پر مرتب ہونے والے اثر یعنی حکم کو بیان فرمایا ہے اور بیع کا حکم ملک ہے پس اس فصل میں بیع فاسد کے مفید ملک ہونے اور نہ ہونے کا بیان ہے یہ خیال رہے کہ ہمارے نزدیک بیع کے اندر فاسد اور باطل دو چیزیں الگ الگ ہیں اور امام شافعی کے نزد یک دونوں ایک چیز ہیں باطل اور فاسد کے درمیان ان کے نزدیک کوئی فرق نہیں ہے۔

صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مشتری نے بیع فاسد کی صورت میں بائع کی اجازت سے مبیع پر قبضہ کر لیا اور عقد مذکور میں دونوں عوض مال ہوں تو مشتری بیع کا مالک ہو جائے گا اور اس پر اس کی قیمت واجب ہوگی۔ یمن واجب نہ ہو گا متن میں فاسد کا لفظ اس لئے ذکر کیا گیا ہے کہ بیع باطل بالکل مفید ملک نہیں ہوتی نہ قبضہ سے پہلے اور نہ قبضہ کے بعد اور امر بالع سے مراد بائع کی اجازت ہے اجازت خواہ صراحتا ہو خواہ دلالتہ اگر بائع نے قبضہ کرنے کی اجازت صراحتاً دی تو مشتری قبضہ کرنے سے مبیع کا مالک ہو جائے گا خواہ بائع کی موجودگی میں قبضہ کیا ہو یا عدم موجودگی میں قبضہ کیا ہو اور دلالۃ اجازت کی صورت یہ ہے کہ مشتری نے مجلس عقد میں بائع کی موجودگی میں قبضہ کیا اور بائع خاموش رہا اور اگر بائع کی عدم موجودگی میں قبضہ کیا تو مشتری مبیع کا مالک نہ ہوگا کیونکہ اس صورت میں نہ صراحتہ اجازت پائی گئی اور نہ دلالۃ اجازت پائی گئی اور دونوں عوض کے مال ہونے کی قید اس لئے لگائی ہے کہ اگر ایک عوض یا دونوں عوض غیر مال ہوں تو بیع باطل ہوگی جیسے بیع بالحرام [؟] اور بیع بالدم اور بیع بالمیتہ۔ بہر حال ہمارے نزدیک بیع فاسد میں مبیع پر مشتری کا قبضہ مفید ملک ہے اور امام شافعی کے نزد یک بیع فاسد میں قبضہ، مفید ملک نہیں ہے یعنی اگر مشتری نے بیع فاسد میں بائع کی اجازت سے بھی قبضہ کیا ہو تب بھی امام شافعی کے نزدیک مشتری مبیع کا مالک نہ ہو گا اس کے قائل امام مالک اور امام احمد ہیں۔

حضرت امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ بیع فاسد ایک حرام طریقہ ہے اور ملکیت کا حاصل ہونا ایک نعمت ہے اور فعل حرام حصول نعمت کا سبب نہیں ہوسکتا کیونکہ سبب اور مسبب کے درمیان مناسبت ضروری ہے اور فعل حرام اور نعمت ملک کے درمیان کوئی مناسبت نہیں ہے بلکہ ایک گونہ تضاد ہے پس جب فعل حرام یعنی بیع فاسد اور نعمت ملک کے درمیان کوئی مناسبت نہیں ہے تو اس فعل حرام یعنی بیع فاسد کے ذریعہ نعمت ملک بھی حاصل نہیں ہوسکتی۔

دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ بیع فاسد کا رواج زمانہ جاہلیت میں تھا لیکن اسلام میں بیع فاسد سے منع کیا گیا ہے تو صاحب شریعت صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے منع کرنے کی وجہ سے بیع فاسد کی مشروعیت منسوخ ہوگئی ہے اور بیع فاسد کی مشروعیت اس لئے منسوخ ہوگئی کہ نہی (ممانعت) اور مشروعیت کے درمیان تضاد اور منافات ہے کیونکہ نہی تقاضہ کرتی ہے فسخ کا اور مشروعیت تقاضہ کرتی ہے حسن کا ۔ اور فسخ اور حسن کے درمیان منافات ہے پس جب حسن اور فسخ کے درمیان منافات ہے تو ان کے مقتضی یعنی نہی اور مشروعیت کے درمیان بھی منافات ہوگی اور جب ان دونوں کے درمیان منافات ہے تو یہ دونوں جمع نہیں ہو سکتے ہیں یعنی یہ نہیں ہو سکتا کہ ایک چیز مشروع بھی ہو اور ممنوع بھی ہو اور جب دونوں جمع نہیں ہو سکتے تو نہی وارد ہونے کے بعد مشروعیت منسوخ ہوگئی پس ثابت ہوا کہ بیع فاسد کی مشروعیت منسوخ ہے یہی وجہ ہے کہ بیع فاسد قبضہ سے پہلے مفید ملک نہیں ہوتی۔ یعنی بیع فاسد اگر مشروع ہوئی تو قبضہ سے پہلے بھی مفید ملک ہوتی جیسا کہ بیع صحیح قبضہ سے پہلے مفید ملک ہوتی ہے پس قبضہ سے پہلے بیع فاسد کا مفید ملک نہ ہونا بھی بیع فاسد کی مشروعیت کے منسوخ ہونے کی دلیل ہے اور جس چیز پر مشروعیت منسوخ ہو جائے وہ چونکہ کسی حکم شرعی کا فائدہ نہیں دیتی اس لئے بیع فاسد مشتری کے واسطے مفید ملک نہ ہوگی اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ کسی نے کوئی چیز مردار کے عوض بیچی یا شراب درہم یا دنانیر کے عوض بیچی اور مشتری نے مبیع پر قبضہ کر لیا تو ان دونوں صورتوں میں احناف کے نزدیک بھی مشتری کے لئے ملک ثابت نہیں ہوتی پس جس طرح ان صورتوں میں بیع کے غیر مشروع ہونے کی وجہ سے بیع مفید ملک نہیں ہوتی اسی طرح بیع فاسدہ کی صورت میں بھی عدم مشروعیت کی وجہ سے بیع فاسد مفید ملک نہ ہوگی حاصل یہ کہ وصار كما اذا باع بالميتة الخ کہ کر امام شافعی نے بیع فاسد کی صورتوں کو بیع باطل کی صورتوں پر قیاس کیا ہے کیونکہ ان کے نزدیک بیع فاسد اور باطل کے درمیان کوئی فرق نہیں ہے۔

ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ بیع فاسد میں یعنی جبکہ مبادلة المال بالمال بالتراضی ہو مگر کوئی شرط فاسد ہو تو ایسی بیع میں بیع کا رکن یعنی ایجاب و قبول ایسے شخصوں سے صادر ہوا ہے جو اس کے اہل ہیں یعنی عاقل ہیں اور بالغ ہیں اور بیع کے محل کی طرف منسوب ہیں یعنی جس چیز کو مبیع بنا دیا گیا ہے وہ مال ہونے کی وجہ سے بیع کا محل بھی ہے پس جب بیع کا رکن یعنی ایجاب وقبول ان کے اہل سے صادر ہوئے اور محل بیع کی طرف سے منسوب ہیں تو بیع کے منعقد ہونے میں کیا اشکال ہے یعنی بیع منعقد ہو جائے گی اور جب بیع منعقد ہوگئی تو مفید ملک ہوگی کیونکہ ہر وہ بیع جس کا رکن اس کے اہل سے صادر ہو اور محل کی طرف منسوب ہو وہ مفید ملک ہوتی ہے اس لئے یہ بیع بھی مفید ملک ہوگی ۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ عاقدین کے اہل ہونے اور مبیع کے محل بیع ہونے میں کوئی خفا نہیں ہے یعنی بائع اور مشتری دونوں میں خرید وفروخت کی لیاقت موجود ہے اور محل بیع یہی مال ہے اور باہمی رضا مندی سے مال سے مال کا مبادلہ کرنا یہی بیع کا رکن ہے اسی مبادلہ کا نام ایجاب وقبول ہے اور ہمارا کلام ایسی ہی بیع فاسد میں ہے جس میں مبادلة المال بالمال پایا جائے یعنی جس میں دونوں عوض مال ہوں چنانچہ اگر دونوں عوض یا ایک عوض مال نہ ہو تو وہ رکن بیع نہ پائے جانے کی وجہ سے بالاتفاق باطل ہے اس کے مفید ملک نہ ہونے میں کسی کا اختلاف نہیں ہے بلکہ بالاجماع غیر مفید ملک ہے حاصل یہ کہ بیع فاسد میں چونکہ رکن بیع یعنی مبادلتہ المال بالمال بالتراضی کو منسوب کیا گیا ہے یعنی مبیع وہ مال ہونے کی وجہ سے محل بیع بھی ہے اس لئے بیع فاسد اپنی ذات کے اعتبار سے مشروع ہوئی اور بیع مشروع مفید ملک ہوتی ہے اس لئے یہ بیع مفید ملک ہو گی۔

والنهي يقرر المشروعية الخ سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔
سوال یہ ہے کہ بیع فاسد پر نہی وارد ہوئی ہے اور نہی مشروع ہونے سے مانع ہے۔ پس نہی وارد ہونے کے باوجود بیع فاسد مشروع کس طرح ہو سکتی ہے۔ جواب ... اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ہمارے نزدیک نہی اگر افعال شرعیہ پر وارد ہو تو وہ فسخ لغیرہ کا تقاضہ کرتی ہے یعنی جس فعل شرعی پر نہی وارد ہوئی ہے وہ اپنی ذات کے اعتبار سے مشروع ہے اسکی ذات میں کوئی قباحت نہیں ہے البتہ غیر یعنی وصف یا اس سے متعلق کسی چیز میں قباحت ہے اور اس کے اعتبار سے وہ غیر مشروع ہے جیسے اذان جمعہ کے وقت بیع کرنا کہ نفس بیع مشروع ہے لیکن وقت اذان کے متصل ہونے سے کراہت پیدا ہوگئی ہے۔ نہی عن الشی چونکہ فعل منہی عنہ کے متصور اور موجود ہونے کا تقاضہ کرتی ہے اس لئے نہی مشروعیت کو منسوخ نہیں کرتی بلکہ مستحکم کر دیتی ہے حاصل یہ ہوا کہ بیع فاسد کی صورت میں نفس بیع مشروع ہے اور شرط مفسد یقیناً ممنوع ہے اور نعمت
Book of Sales. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah, Volume Eight. In the case of wine, only price is obligatory and price can be a countervalue; it cannot be sold. Explanation: Ahkam is plural of hukm, and hukm is the effect attached to a thing, and the effect occurs after the thing. Therefore, first the thing, i.e., the fasid sale, is mentioned, and then the effect attached to it, i.e., the ruling, is explained. And the ruling of sale is ownership. So this chapter discusses whether a fasid sale confers ownership or not. Note that according to us, in sale, fasid and batil are two separate things. According to Imam Shafi'i, both are one thing; there is no difference between batil and fasid in his view. The issue is: if the buyer takes possession of the sold item with the seller's permission in a fasid sale, and in the mentioned contract both countervalues are property (mal), then the buyer becomes owner of the sale and its price becomes obligatory upon him. The word 'fasid' is mentioned in the text because a batil sale does not confer ownership at all, neither before possession nor after. And 'amr bil-qabd' means the seller's permission, whether explicitly or implicitly. If the seller explicitly permits taking possession, then the buyer becomes owner of the sold item by taking possession, whether he takes possession in the seller's presence or absence. The implicit permission is when the buyer takes possession in the seller's presence in the assembly of contract and the seller remains silent. But if he takes possession in the seller's absence, the buyer does not become owner, because neither explicit nor implicit permission is found. The condition that both countervalues be property is stipulated because if one or both countervalues are non-property, the sale is batil — like the sale of a corpse, sale of blood, and sale of carrion. Nevertheless, according to us, in a fasid sale, the buyer's possession of the sold item confers ownership. According to Imam Shafi'i, possession in a fasid sale does not confer ownership, meaning even if the buyer takes possession with the seller's permission, according to Imam Shafi'i, the buyer does not become owner. Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad also hold this view. Imam Shafi'i's proof is that a fasid sale is a prohibited way, and acquiring ownership is a blessing. A prohibited act cannot be a cause for obtaining a blessing, because there must be appropriateness between cause and effect, and there is no appropriateness between a prohibited act and the blessing of ownership; rather there is a kind of contradiction. So since there is no appropriateness, the blessing of ownership cannot be obtained through this prohibited act. His second proof is that fasid sale was prevalent in the Jahiliyyah, but in Islam it was prohibited. So due to the prohibition by the Lawgiver (peace be upon him), the legitimacy of fasid sale is abrogated. Its legitimacy is abrogated because there is contradiction and opposition between prohibition (nahy) and legitimacy (mashru'iyyah). Since nahy demands annulment (faskh) and legitimacy demands goodness (husn), and there is opposition between annulment and goodness, there is also opposition between their implications. When these two are in opposition, they cannot be combined, meaning a thing cannot be both legitimate and prohibited. When they cannot combine, after the prohibition, legitimacy is abrogated. Thus it is established that the legitimacy of fasid sale is abrogated. This is why a fasid sale does not confer ownership before possession. If it were legitimate, it would confer ownership even before possession, just as a valid sale confers ownership before possession. So the fact that a fasid sale does not confer ownership before possession is also evidence of the abrogation of its legitimacy. And when a thing's legitimacy is abrogated, it does not give any legal benefit, therefore the fasid sale will not confer ownership for the buyer. This is like if someone sells a thing in exchange for carrion, or sells wine for dirhams or dinars, and the buyer takes possession — in these two cases, even according to the Hanafis, ownership is not established for the buyer. So just as in those cases the sale does not confer ownership due to its illegitimacy, similarly in the case of fasid sale, due to lack of legitimacy, it will not confer ownership. In summary, he said 'and it becomes as if he sold for carrion, etc.' — Imam Shafi'i analogized cases of fasid sale to cases of batil sale because in his view there is no difference between fasid and batil. Our proof is that in a fasid sale, i.e., when there is an exchange of property with mutual consent but with some invalid condition, the pillar of sale, i.e., offer and acceptance, emanates from persons who are qualified for it — they are sane and adult, and it is attributed to the subject matter of sale — that is, the thing made into the sold item is property and thus a valid subject matter. So when the pillar of sale issues from qualified persons and is attributed to the subject matter, there is no objection to the sale being concluded. It will be concluded, and when concluded, it confers ownership, because every sale whose pillar issues from qualified persons and is attributed to the subject matter confers ownership. Therefore this sale also confers ownership. The author of Hidayah says there is no concealment in the qualification of the contracting parties and the sold item being the subject matter, meaning both seller and buyer have the capacity to transact, and the subject matter is property. The mutual exchange of property with mutual consent is the pillar of sale. This exchange is called offer and acceptance. Our discussion is in such a fasid sale where exchange of property is found, i.e., where both countervalues are property. If both or one countervalue is non-property, then due to the absence of the pillar, it is batil by consensus — there is no disagreement that it does not confer ownership; rather it is unanimously non-proprietary. In summary, in a fasid sale, since the pillar of sale — exchange of property with mutual consent — is attributed, i.e., the sold item is property and thus a valid subject matter, therefore the fasid sale is legitimate in its essence, and a legitimate sale confers ownership, so this sale will confer ownership. The phrase 'And prohibition establishes legitimacy' etc. is an answer to a question. The question is: A prohibition has been issued regarding fasid sale, and prohibition prevents legitimacy. So despite the prohibition, how can fasid sale be legitimate? The answer: According to us, when a prohibition is issued on Shari'ah acts, it demands nullification due to something external (faskh li-ghayrihi), meaning the act upon which the prohibition is issued is legitimate in its essence; there is no defect in its essence. Rather, the defect is external — an attribute or something related to it — and due to that it is non-legitimate. For example, selling at the time of Friday adhan — the sale itself is legitimate, but due to its conjunction with the adhan time, it becomes makruh. Since prohibition of a thing demands that the prohibited act be conceivable and existent, prohibition does not abrogate legitimacy; rather it strengthens it. The conclusion is that in the case of fasid sale, the sale itself is legitimate, and the corrupting condition is certainly prohibited. And the blessing...
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of Ashraf al-Hidayah addresses a key intra-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i (with agreement from Imams Malik and Ahmad). The topic is whether a fasid (defective) sale transfers ownership of the sold item to the buyer when he takes possession with the seller's permission. The author begins by clarifying the terminology: in Hanafi fiqh, 'fasid' and 'batil' are distinct categories—fasid is valid in essence but has a defect in a condition, while batil is fundamentally invalid. Imam al-Shafi'i equates them. The ruling stated: if both countervalues are property (mal) and the buyer takes possession with the seller's permission (explicit or implicit), the buyer becomes owner and must pay the market price (not the named price). Implicit permission is when the buyer takes possession in the seller's presence during the council of contract and the seller remains silent. If possession occurs in the seller's absence without explicit permission, ownership does not transfer. This condition distinguishes fasid from batil sales, where possession never transfers ownership. The Shafi'i position is presented with two rational proofs. First, a fasid sale is a prohibited act (haram), and obtaining ownership (a blessing) cannot result from a prohibited act due to lack of appropriateness (munasaba) between cause and effect—rather there is a contradiction. Second, the prohibition (nahy) from the Prophet (peace be upon him) abrogates the legitimacy of fasid sale; since a prohibited act cannot be legitimate, the sale cannot confer ownership. The Shafi'i also analogizes fasid sales to batil sales like selling carrion or wine, where even Hanafis agree ownership does not transfer. The Hanafi response rebuts both proofs. For the first, the Hanafis argue that the pillar of sale (offer and acceptance) is validly performed by qualified parties, and the subject matter is property, so the contract is concluded and ownership transfers by virtue of the contract's validity. The prohibition on the fasid element (e.g., an invalid condition) does not negate the essence of the contract. For the second objection, the Hanafis use the principle 'nahy yugariru al-mashru'iyyah' (prohibition confirms legitimacy). They explain that when prohibition attaches to a shar'i act, it indicates that the act's essence is legitimate; the prohibition only cancels an external attribute (faskh li-ghayrihi). An example is selling during Friday adhan: the sale is legitimate in itself but becomes makruh due to the time. Thus, the prohibition does not abrogate the contract's basic validity but rather strengthens it. The passage concludes that the essence of the fasid sale is legitimate, and only the corrupting condition is prohibited. This aligns with the Hanafi principle that a fasid contract can confer ownership upon possession (with permission), unlike a batil contract which never does.

Key Terms
بیع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is valid in its essence (valid contract of exchange) but has a defect in a condition, e.g., an invalid stipulation.
بیع باطل (bay' batil): A sale lacking an essential pillar, e.g., the subject matter is not property or the parties lack capacity.
نہی (nahy): A prohibition from the Lawgiver; here, the prohibition on fasid sales due to the corrupt condition.
مشروعیت (mashru'iyyah): The legality or legitimacy of an act in Shari'ah; here, the fasid sale is mashru' in its essence according to Hanafis.
ملک (milk): Ownership; the legal consequence of a valid sale. In fasid sale, ownership transfers upon possession with permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a buyer become owner of the sold item in a fasid sale?
A) Immediately upon concluding the contract
B) Upon taking possession with the seller's permission
C) Never, because the sale is invalid
D) Only if the seller explicitly states the permission after the contract

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the Hanafi school holds that the contract's essence is valid and ownership transfers upon possession with permission, because the prohibition only targets the external defect, not the core exchange.

Ownership in Voidable Sale: Before and After Possession
Inter-madhhab dispute on ownership in voidable sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership in a voidable sale before and after possession and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
In a voidable sale, ownership transfers only after possession—possession strengthens the weak cause.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on when ownership transfers in a voidable sale (bay' fasid). While the contract is valid in its pillars, the presence of an invalid condition makes it 'weak'. The Hanafi school holds that ownership does not transfer immediately but only after the buyer takes possession. This ruling is defended against three analogies by Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues that a voidable sale should produce no ownership at all. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi distinction between fasid (voidable) and batil (void) contracts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that ownership transfers only after possession, not at contract.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection and its three analogies
Note the three analogies: before vs after possession, carrion sale, wine sale.
3
Follow the Hanafi response to each analogy
For each, understand why the analogy fails: strengthening corruption, missing pillar, dishonored property.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: buyer cannot sell before possession, can after; seller must return price.

کا حاصل یہ ہے۔ کہ امام شافعی کا بیع فاسد میں مابعد القبض کو ماقبل القبض پر قیاس کرنا درست نہیں ہے یعنی یہ کہنا درست نہیں ہے کہ بیع فاسد جس طرح صحیح پر قبضہ مشتری سے پہلے مفید ملک نہیں ہے اسی طرح قبضہ واپس لیکر فساد دور کرنا واجب ہے تو مشتری کا مبیع سپرد کرنے کا مطالبہ سے رک کر فساد دور کرنا بدرجہ اولی واجب ہوگا کیونکہ مشتری کا مبیع سپرد کرنے کا مطالبہ سے رک جانا آسان ہے بہ نسبت اس کے کہ مشتری مبیع
