بھی مشتری کے واسطے ملک ثابت کرنے سے فساد مستحکم ہو جاتا ہے لہذا قبضہ مبیع کے بعد مشتری کے لئے ملک مبیع ثابت نہ ہونا چاہئے تھی تا کہ فساد متحکم نہ ہو جیہ ا کہ اس اندیشہ سے قبضہ سے پہلے ملک ثابت نہیں ہوتی۔
جواب ... اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ قبضہ کے بعد مشتری کے لئے ملک اس لئے ثابت ہو جاتی ہے کہ جب مشتری نے مبیع پر قبضہ کر لیا تو صبیع مشتری کے ضمان میں داخل ہو گئی یعنی اگر مبیع مشتری کے قبضہ سے ضائع ہوگئی تو مشتری اس کا ضامن ہوگا پس جب مبیع مشتری کے ضمان میں داخل ہو گئی اور بائع اس کے عوض شمن کا مالک ہو گیا تو اب اگر ملک مبیع بائع سے مشتری کی طرف منتقل نہ ہو تو دونوں بدل یعنی بیع اور شمن ایک شخص یعنی بائع کی ملک میں جمع ہو جائیں گے اور سابق میں گذر چکا کہ دونوں عوضین کا ایک شخص کی ملک میں جمع ہونا نا جائز ہے اس لئے کہا گیا کہ قبضہ کرنے کے بعد ملک مبیع مشتری کے لئے ثابت ہو جائے گی اور قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے چونکہ دونوں عوضین کا ایک شخص کی ملک میں جمع ہونا لازم نہیں آتا اس لئے قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے ملک مبیع مشتری کے لئے ثابت نہ ہوگی۔ اور بیع فاسد کے قبل القبض مفید ملک نہ ہونے اور بعد القبض مفید ملک ہونے کی دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ ملک کا سبب یعنی بیع فاسد شرط فاسد یعنی امر قبیح کے مقترن ہونے کی وجہ سے کمزور ہے اور کمزور چیز پر اس کا حکم مرتب نہیں ہوتا تا وقتیکہ اس کے ساتھ کوئی طاقتور چیز نہ لگ جائے پس یہاں طاقتور چیز قبضہ ہے اس لئے اگر بیع فاسد میں مشتری کا مبیع پر قبضہ پایا گیا تو بیع فاسد پر اس کا حکم مرتب ہو جائے گا یعنی مشتری کی ملک ثابت ہو جائے گی اور اگر قبضہ نہیں پایا گیا تو بیع فاسد پر اس کا حکم مرتب نہ ہو گا یعنی مشتری کی ملک ثابت نہ ہوگی اور یہ ایسا ہے جیسا کہ ہبہ میں اگر موہوب لہ نے شئی موہوب پر قبضہ کر لیا تو ہبہ تام ہو جائے گا اور موہوب لہ کی ملک ثابت ہوگی اور اگر موہوب لہ نے شئی موہوب پر قبضہ نہیں کیا تو ہبہ تام نہ ہو گا اور موہوب لہ شئی موہوب کا مالک نہ ہوگا۔
امام شافعی کے قیاس ثانی کا جواب ... والميتة ليست بمال الخ سے امام شافعی کے قیاس وصار كما اذا باع بالميتة کا جواب ہے جواب یہ ہے کہ مردار چونکہ مال نہیں ہے اسلئے بیع بالمیتہ کی صورت میں احد العوضین یعنی شمن مال نہ ہوا اور جب احد العوضین مال نہیں ہے تو رکن بیع یعنی مبادلہ المال بالمال معدوم ہو گیا اور جب بیع کا رکن معدوم ہو گیا تو بیع منعقد ہی نہیں ہوئی ہے اور جب بیع منعقد نہیں ہوئی تو وہ مفید ملک بھی نہ ہوگی نہ قبضہ سے پہلے اور نہ قبضہ کے بعد اس کے برخلاف بیع فاسد کہ وہ منعقد ہو جاتی ہے لیکن شرط فاسد کی وجہ سے اس میں فساد پیدا ہو جاتا ہے پس اس قدر فرق کے ساتھ بیع فاسد کو بیع بالمیہ (بیع باطل) پر قیاس کرنا درست نہ ہوگا۔ امام شافعی کے قیاس ثالث کا جواب .. ولو كان الخمر الخ سے امام شافعی کے قیاس باع الخمر بالدرہم کا جواب ہے۔ جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ عقد بیع میں اگر خمر مبیع ہو جیسا کہ امام شافعی نے کہا ہے تو اس کی تخریج ہم بیع فاسد کے باب کے شروع میں کر چکے ہیں کہ مبیع عقد بیع میں چونکہ مقصود ہوتی ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں شراب مقصود اور قابل اعزاز ہو گی حالانکہ شریعت نے اس کی توہین کا حکم کیا ہے اس وجہ سے بیع الخمر بالدراہم باطل ہوگی۔ اور ہمارا کلام بیع فاسد میں ہے پس فاسد اور باطل کے درمیان چونکہ ہمارے نزدیک بہت بڑا فرق ہے اس لئے بیع فاسد کو بیع باطل پر قیاس کرنا درست نہ ہوگا اور بیع الخمر بالدراہم کے باطل ہونے کی دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر شراب کی بیع منعقد ہو جائے تو بائع پر شراب کی قیمت واجب ہوگی کیونکہ مسلمان شراب کو نہ سپرد کر سکتا ہے اور نہ اس پر قبضہ کر سکتا ہے اور یہ امر مسلم ہے کہ قیمت شمن ہوتی ہے مبیع نہیں ہوتی پس اگر ہم بیع الخمر بالدراہم کی صورت میں انعقاد بیع کے قائل ہو جائیں تو قیمت شمن ہو جائے گی کیونکہ دراہم ودنانیر کے مقابلہ میں جو چیز ہوتی ہے وہ بیع ہوتی ہے پس شراب کی قیمت جو بائع پر واجب ہوئی ہے مبیع ہوئی حالانکہ شریعت نے قیمت کو شمن بنایا تھا نہ کہ مبیع پس اس صورت میں امر مشروع کو متغیر کرنا لازم آیا اور امر مشروع کو متغیر کرنا بھی ناجائز اور باطل ہے اس لئے بیع الخمر بالدراہم باطل ہوئی اور بیع فاسد کو بیع باطل پر فارق کی وجہ سے قیاس کرنا چونکہ جائز نہیں ہے اس لئے بیع فاسد کی ورتوں کو بیع الخمر بالدراہم پر قیاس کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔
بائع کے امر سے مراد اجازت ہے
ثم شرط ان يكون القبض باذن البائع وهو الظاهر الا انه يكتفى به دلالة كما اذا قبضه في مجلس العقد الشحات وهو الصحيح لان البيع تسليط منه على القبض فاذا قبضه بحضرته قبل الافتراق ولم ينهه كان بحكم تسليط السابق وكذا القبض فى الهبة فى مجلس العقد يصح استحسانا وشرطه ان يكون في العقد عرضان كل واحد منهما مال ليتحقق ركن البيع هو مبادلة المال بالمال فيخرج عليه البيع بالميتة والدم و سحرو الريح والبيع مع نفى الثمن وقوله لزمته قيمته فى ذوات القيم فاما فى ذوات الامثال يلزمه المثل لانه مضمون بنفسه بالقبض فشابه الغصب وهذا لان المثل صورة و معنى اعدل من المثل معنى
ترجمہ پھر ( قدوری میں یہ شرط لگائی کہ قبضہ بائع کی اجازت سے ہوا اور یہی ظاہر الروایہ ہے مگر دلالۃً اجازت پر اکتفاء کیا جاتا ہے جیسا کہ مجلس عقد میں ( بائع کے روبرو) مشتری نے مبیع پر قبضہ کر لیا تو استحسانا ( جائز ہے ) اور یہی صحیح ہے کیونکہ بیع کرنا بائع کی طرف سے قبضہ
Book of Sales... Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary volume... The permissibility of a voidable sale is sufficient for obtaining ownership; thus by the sale itself the buyer acquires ownership. And when ownership is acquired by the sale, it is not necessary that the blessing of ownership be obtained through a prohibited act, as Imam al-Shafi'i stated. Response to Imam Shafi'i's first analogy: ... The response to Imam Shafi'i's analogy 'hence it does not benefit before possession' is that his analogy equating after-possession to before-possession in a voidable sale is incorrect. Because if ownership were established for the buyer before possession, the buyer would be obligated to deliver the price and the seller obligated to deliver the sold item, as these are effects of the contract; and delivering the price and the item would strengthen the corruption (fasād), which is impermissible. The corruption is removed by taking back the item from the buyer after possession; therefore strengthening it is not allowed. So we say ownership is not established before possession. A question arises: If establishing ownership before possession strengthens the corruption, then establishing it after possession would also strengthen it, so ownership should not be established even after possession. The answer: After possession, the sold item enters the buyer's liability (ḍamān); if it perishes, the buyer is liable. Thus the item is in the buyer's liability and the seller becomes owner of the price; if ownership does not transfer to the buyer, then both countervalues (item and price) would be owned by the seller, which is not allowed. Therefore ownership is established after possession. Another proof: The cause of ownership (the voidable sale) is weak due to an invalid condition; it does not produce its effect until joined by a strong element, which is possession. It is like a gift (hiba): if the donee takes possession, the gift is complete and ownership transfers; otherwise not. Response to Imam Shafi'i's second analogy: ... The sale of carrion (meytah) is invalid because carrion is not property; thus one countervalue (the price) is not property, so the pillar of sale (exchange of property for property) is missing, and the contract does not exist. A voidable sale does exist but has a defect; so analogy with void sale is invalid. Response to Imam Shafi'i's third analogy: ... The sale of wine (khamr) for dirhams is void (bāṭil) because wine is dishonored by Shariah; if it were valid, the seller would owe the value of wine, which would become the sold item, whereas value (qīmah) is meant to be the price, not the sold item; this would change the Shariah ruling. So analogy between voidable and void is invalid. Then condition that possession be with seller's permission, but implied permission suffices, e.g., in the session of contract. And the pillar of sale is exchange of property for property, excluding sales of carrion, blood, wine, etc. And he said: he owes its value for things of value, and for things of like (mithliyyāt) he owes the like, because it is guaranteed by possession, similar to usurpation; and the like is more just both in form and meaning. Translation: Then (in al-Quduri) this condition is laid that possession be with seller's permission, and this is the apparent transmission, but implicit permission suffices, as when the buyer takes possession in the session of contract before separation, and the seller does not forbid; it is valid by istiḥsān, and this is correct because the sale itself is authorization for possession.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding ownership in a voidable sale (bay' fasid). The Hanafi position is presented and defended against three analogies of Imam al-Shafi'i. The passage begins by stating that in a voidable sale, ownership of the sold item transfers to the buyer by the sale itself, not by any prohibited act. However, this ownership is suspended until possession (qabd). The first Shafi'i analogy claims that since ownership does not transfer before possession, it should not transfer after possession either. The Hanafi response explains that before possession, establishing ownership would force both parties to fulfill obligations (delivery of price and item) which would strengthen the corrupt contract, impermissible. After possession, non-transfer would lead to both countervalues remaining with the seller, also impermissible. Furthermore, possession acts as a strengthener for the weak cause (the corrupt contract), similar to a gift (hiba) requiring possession. The second Shafi'i analogy equates a voidable sale to a void sale (like carrion). The Hanafi rebuttal highlights that a void sale lacks the pillar of exchange of property, whereas a voidable sale has the pillar but with a defect. The third analogy involves the sale of wine, which is absolutely void (batil) because wine is not worthy of consideration. The distinction between fasid (voidable) and batil (void) is crucial: fasid contracts exist but have a remediable defect, while batil contracts do not exist. The passage concludes with a condition that possession must be with the seller's permission, but implied permission suffices, especially in the session of contract. It also mentions the pillar of sale (mubadala al-mal bil-mal) excluding non-property items. Finally, it notes that after possession, the buyer owes the like (mithl) for fungibles and value (qimah) for non-fungibles, similar to usurpation, because possession creates liability.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is valid in its pillars (offer, acceptance, and property exchange) but contains a defect such as an invalid condition or lack of possession at contract; it becomes effective only after possession.
قبض (qabd): Physical or constructive possession of the sold item, which in a voidable sale is necessary for ownership to transfer.
مبادلة المال بالمال (mubadala al-mal bil-mal): The exchange of property for property; considered the essential pillar (rukn) of a sale

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what effect does possession (qabd) have on a voidable sale?
A) It perfects the sale and ownership transfers retroactively.
B) It strengthens the weak cause, causing ownership to transfer from that point.
C) It has no effect because the sale is void.
D) It makes the seller liable for the price.

Key Takeaway
In a voidable sale, ownership transfers only after possession, distinguishing fasid from batil and preventing both strengthening corruption and dual ownership.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Refuting the Shafi'i Qiyas on Fasid and Batil Sales
Refuting Imam Shafi'i's analogies between fasid and batil sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi distinction between fasid and batil sales and refute the Shafi'i analogy that equates them.

Core Idea
Fasid sales are concluded but defective; batil sales lack a pillar entirely — the two cannot be analogized.

This passage addresses a critical methodological dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the classification of invalid sales. Imam al-Shafi'i analogized fasid (defective) sales to batil (void) sales to derive similar legal consequences. The Hanafi commentator firmly rejects this analogy by showing that the two categories differ fundamentally: a fasid sale has a valid pillar (rukn) — both exchange items are property — but is defective due to a condition, while a batil sale lacks the pillar entirely because one exchange item is not property. This lesson will walk through the Hanafi refutation and clarify the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on fasid and batil sales
Identify the defining characteristic of each: fasid sales are concluded (mun'aqid) but defective due to an illegal condition; batil sales are void ab initio because an essential pillar is missing.
2
Identify Imam Shafi'i's analogies
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i compared a fasid sale to a sale of a dead animal or wine, arguing that both are invalid and should have similar rulings.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to each analogy
Examine why each analogy fails: dead animal is not property (mal), so the pillar of exchange of property for property is absent; wine is not honored in Shari'ah and cannot be subject matter. In both cases, the sale is batil, not fasid.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Understand the real-world effect: in a fasid sale, possession with permission can transfer ownership; in a batil sale, possession never transfers ownership.

امام شافعی کے قیاس نانی کا جواب ... والميتة ليست بمال الخ سے امام شافعی کے قیاس وصار كما اذا باع بالمينة کا جواب ہے جواب یہ ہے کہ مردار چونکہ مال نہیں ہے اسلئے بیع بالمیتہ کی صورت میں احد العوضین یعنی شمن مال نہ ہوا اور جب احد العونین مال نہیں ہے تو رکن بیع یعنی مبادلہ المال بالمال معدوم ہو گیا اور جب بیچ کا رکن معدوم ہو گیا تو بیع منعقد ہی نہیں ہوئی ہے اور جب بیچ منعقد نہیں ہوئی تو وہ مفید ملک بھی نہ ہوگی نہ قبضہ سے پہلے اور نہ قبضہ کے بعد اس کے برخلاف بیع فاسد کہ وہ منعقد ہو جاتی ہے لیکن شرط فاسد کی وجہ سے اس میں فساد پیدا ہو جاتا ہے پس اس قدر فرق کے ساتھ بیع فاسد کو بیع بالمیہ (بیع باطل ) پر قیاس کرنا درست نہ ہوگا۔ امام شافعی کے قیاس ثالث کا جواب .. ولو كان الخمر الخ سے امام شافعی کے قیاس باع الخمر بالدرہم کا جواب ہے۔ جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ عقد بیع میں اگر خم مبیع ہو جیسا کہ امام شافعی نے کہا ہے تو اس کی تخریج ہم بیع فاسد کے باب کے شروع میں کر چکے ہیں کہ مبیع عقد بیع میں چونکہ مقصود ہوتی ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں شراب مقصود اور قابل اعزاز ہو گی حالانکہ شریعت نے اس کی توہین کا حکم کیا ہے اس وجہ سے بیع الخمر بالدراہم باطل ہوگی ۔ اور ہمارا کلام بیع فاسد میں ہے پس فاسد اور باطل کے درمیان چونکہ ہمارے نزد یک بہت بڑا فرق ہے اس لئے بیع فاسد کو بیع باطل پر قیاس کرنا درست نہ ہوگا اور بیع الخمر بالدراہم کے باطل ہونے کی دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر شراب کی بیع منعقد ہو جائے تو بائع پر شراب کی قیمت واجب ہوگی کیونکہ مسلمان شراب کونہ سپر د کر سکتا ہے اور نہ اس پر قبضہ کر سکتا ہے اور یہ مر مسلم ہے کہ قیمت شمن ہوتی ہے مبیع نہیں ہوتی پس اگر ہم بیع النمر بالدراہم کی صورت میں انعقاد بیع کے قائل ہو جائیں تو قیمت شر میچ ہو جائے گی کیونکہ دراہم ودنا نیر کے مقابلہ میں جو چیز ہوتی ہے وہ بیچ ہوتی ہے پس شراب کی قیمت جو بائع پر واجب ہوئی ہے مبیع ہوئی ۔ مالانکہ شریعت نے قیمت کو شمن بنایا تھانہ کہ مبیع پس اس صورت میں امر مشروع کو متغیر کرنا لازم آیا اور امر مشروع کو متغیر کرنا بھی ناجائز اور طل ہے اس لئے بیع النمر بالدراہم باطل ہوئی اور بیع فاسد کو بیع بال پر فارق کی وجہ سے قیاس کرنا چونکہ جائز نہیں ہے اس لئے بیع فاسد کی ورتوں کو بی اثر با مدرا ہم پر قیاس کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔
Refutation of Imam Shafi'i's first analogy: From 'And the dead animal is not property' is the response to Imam Shafi'i's analogy 'and it becomes as if he sold for a dead animal'. The answer is that since a dead animal is not property, in the case of selling for a dead animal, one of the two exchange items, i.e., the price, is not property. And when one of the two exchange items is not property, the pillar of sale, i.e., exchange of property for property, ceases to exist. And when the pillar of sale ceases to exist, the sale is not concluded at all. And when the sale is not concluded, it is not effective for ownership, neither before nor after possession. Contrary to a fasid sale, which is concluded, but due to a defective condition, defect arises in it. Therefore, with such a difference, analogizing a fasid sale to a sale for a dead animal (a batil sale) is not correct. Refutation of Imam Shafi'i's third analogy: From 'and if wine were [sold]...' is the response to Imam Shafi'i's analogy of selling wine for dirhams. The summary of the answer is that in a sales contract, if wine is the subject matter, as Imam Shafi'i argued, then we have already discussed its derivation at the beginning of the chapter on fasid sales: that since the subject matter in a sales contract is intended, in this case wine would be intended and honored, whereas the Shari'ah has commanded its dishonor. For this reason, selling wine for dirhams is batil. And our discussion is about fasid sales. So since between fasid and batil there is a great difference according to us, analogizing a fasid sale to a batil sale is not correct. Another proof of the batil nature of selling wine for dirhams is that if the sale of wine were concluded, the seller would be liable for the price of the wine, because a Muslim can neither deliver wine nor take possession of it. And it is a well-known principle that the price (qimah) is the exchange (thaman), not the subject matter (mabi'). So if we accept the conclusion of the sale in the case of selling wine for dirhams, then the price would become the subject matter, because what is exchanged for dirhams and dinars is the subject matter. Thus, the price of wine, which becomes obligatory on the seller, would be the subject matter, whereas the Shari'ah made the price the exchange, not the subject matter. In this case, changing what is prescribed (mashru') becomes necessary, and changing what is prescribed is impermissible and void. Therefore, selling wine for dirhams is batil. And analogizing a fasid sale to a batil sale is not permissible due to the difference, so analogizing the rulings of a fasid sale to a batil sale is not permissible.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute where the Hanafi commentator refutes Imam Shafi'i's analogies (qiyas) equating fasid and batil sales. The text addresses three specific analogies that Imam Shafi'i is reported to have made. The first analogy compares a sale for a dead animal (murdar) to a fasid sale. The Hanafi response argues that a dead animal is not property (mal), so one of the exchange items in the sale is missing. This means the fundamental pillar (rukn) of sale—exchange of property for property (mubadalah al-mal bil-mal)—is absent, rendering the sale non-existent (batil). A fasid sale, in contrast, has both exchange items as property, but it is defective due to an illegal condition; therefore, it is still 'concluded' (mun'aqid) though invalid. The second analogy involves selling wine for dirhams. The commentator repeats that wine cannot be the subject matter of a sale because it is not honored in Shari'ah; selling it is batil. The main point is that a fasid sale is a valid contract in its essence (because both property items exist) but is rendered defective by an external condition. Therefore, it cannot be compared to a batil sale where the contract itself is void. The Hanafi position consistently maintains this distinction, which affects how ownership transfers: in a fasid sale, possession with the seller's permission can transfer ownership, whereas in a batil sale, possession never transfers ownership.

Key Terms
بیع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is concluded (mun'aqid) in its essential pillars but is rendered defective due to an illegal condition; ownership can be perfected through possession with permission.
بیع باطل (bay' batil): A sale that lacks an essential pillar (rukn) or condition, e.g., one exchange item not being property; it is void and never transfers ownership.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') based on a common effective cause ('illah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school consider a sale for a dead animal (murdar) batil rather than fasid?
A) Because dead animal is najis and cannot be sold
B) Because dead animal is not considered property (mal), so the pillar of sale (exchange of property for property) is missing
C) Because the condition of the sale is defective
D) Because the seller does not own the dead animal

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between fasid and batil sales is not a technicality but a fundamental difference in the pillar of the contract.

Possession in Fasid Sale: Permission and Mal Conditions
Explains conditions for qabd in fasid sale to transfer ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for possession in a fasid sale to transfer ownership and apply the Hanafi rule that implicit permission in the majlis suffices by istihsan.

Core Idea
Possession in a fasid sale transfers ownership only with the seller's permission — implicit in the session suffices.

This passage from Al-Hidayah explains the conditions that make possession (qabd) in a fasid sale effective in transferring ownership. The Hanafi school, drawing on istihsan, holds that explicit permission is not always required; taking the item in the seller's presence in the contract session without objection is sufficient. Additionally, the school requires both exchanged items to be property (mal) for the sale to be concluded. Finally, the passage outlines compensation when such a sale is consummated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
State the Hanafi ruling: possession in a fasid sale transfers ownership only if taken with the seller's permission. What are the two conditions? (1) permission, (2) both items must be mal.
The ruling is not about the validity of the sale itself but about the effect of possession.
2
Analyze the proof for implicit permission
The proof is istihsan citing 'the sale is an authorization (taslit) for possession.' Explain how this implies permission when the buyer takes in the seller's presence without objection.
This contrasts with qiyas that might require explicit permission.
3
Distinguish between explicit and implicit permission
Zahir al-riwayah requires explicit permission, but the preferred view (mufta bihi) accepts implicit permission in the majlis. Apply this rule to fasid sales and gifts.
Implicit permission is derived from the seller's silence and the context of the majlis.
4
Determine the condition of mal
Both the sold item and the price must be mal (property). Identify which sales are excluded: dead meat, blood, free person, air, sale without price. These are batil because the pillar (exchange of mal for mal) is absent.
If one item is not mal, the sale is void ab initio.
5
Calculate compensation for consummated fasid sale
If possession has occurred in a fasid sale, the buyer must pay compensation. Distinguish between dhawat al-qiyam (value based) and dhawat al-amthal (like based). Explain the rationale: possession creates liability resembling ghasb.
For fungibles, mithl is more equitable than qimah according to the Hanafi school.

Source Text

Then the condition is that possession (qabd) be with the permission of the seller (ba'i'), and that is the apparent (zahir al-riwayah), except that implicit (dilalah) permission is sufficient, as when he takes possession in the session of the contract (majlis al-'aqd) before separation, and this is correct, because the sale is an authorization (taslit) from the seller for possession. So if he takes possession in his presence before parting and he (the seller) does not forbid him, it is by virtue of the prior authorization. Similarly, possession in a gift (hibah) in the session of the contract is valid by istihsan. And its condition is that in the contract there be two exchange items, each of which is property (mal), so that the pillar of sale (rukn al-bay')—which is the exchange of property for property (mubadalah al-mal bil-mal)—is realized. Thereupon, the sale of dead meat, blood, magic, air, and the sale with negation of price are excluded. And his (the author's) statement: 'He is liable for its value (qimah) for things of value (dhawat al-qiyam). But for things of standard measure (dhawat al-amthal), the like (mithl) is obligatory upon him, because it is guaranteed (mamnun) by its very possession, so it resembles usurpation (ghasb). And this is because the like in form and meaning is more equitable than the similar in meaning only.' Translation: Then (in al-Quduri) this condition is mentioned: that possession occurs with the permission of the seller, and that is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah). But implicit permission is sufficient, as when in the session of the contract (in the presence of the seller) the buyer takes possession of the sold item, then by istihsan it is valid, and this is correct. Because making a sale is giving the buyer the ability to take possession from the seller's side. So when in the seller's presence the buyer takes possession before separating and the seller does not forbid him, this possession will be by virtue of the prior authorization. Similarly, in the case of a gift, taking possession in the session of the gift (majlis al-hibah) without explicit permission is valid by istihsan. And (in al-Quduri) there is also the condition that in the contract of sale both exchange items are property, so that the pillar of sale, i.e., exchange of property for property, is realized. So upon this condition, the sale of dead meat, blood, a free person, air, and the sale with negation of price are excluded. And the author's statement: 'He is liable for its value (qimah) in things of value (dhawat al-qiyam). And in things of standard measure (dhawat al-amthal), the like (mithl) is obligatory upon him, because by possession the item is guaranteed in itself (mamnun bi-nafsihi), so it resembles usurpation (ghasb). And this is because the like in form and meaning is more equitable than the similar in meaning only.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil) from the Hanafi school, taken from the commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. It discusses two conditions for possession (qabd) in a fasid sale to transfer ownership. First, possession must be with the seller's permission (idhn). The default (zahir al-riwayah) is explicit permission, but implicit (dilalah) permission suffices—specifically when the buyer takes possession in the session of the contract (majlis al-'aqd) in the seller's presence and the seller does not object. This is justified by istihsan because the act of concluding the sale implies authorization (taslit) for the buyer to take possession. The same applies to gifts (hibah): possession in the gift session without explicit permission is valid by istihsan. Second, both exchange items (the sold item and the price) must be property (mal). This is essential because the pillar (rukn) of sale is the exchange of property for property (mubadalah al-mal bil-mal). If either item is not mal—e.g., dead meat, blood, a free person, air, or if the price is negated—the sale is batil (void). Finally, the passage discusses the compensation when a fasid sale is consummated by possession: for items of value (dhawat al-qiyam, i.e., non-fungible goods), the buyer pays the market value (qimah); for fungible goods (dhawat al-amthal), he pays the like (mithl). The reasoning is that once possession occurs, the buyer becomes liable (mamnun) for the item, resembling the liability in usurpation (ghasb). Paying the exact equivalent (mithl) is more equitable than an approximate value.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Possession; physical taking of the sold item by the buyer.
اذن (idhn): Permission; explicit or implicit authorization from the seller for the buyer to take possession.
مجلس العقد (majlis al-'aqd): The session of the contract; the time and place where the sale agreement is made before the parties separate.
مال (mal): Property; something of value that can be lawfully owned and traded.
ذوات القيم (dhawat al-qiyam): Items of value that are not fungible; commodities for which the market value (qimah) is paid in compensation.
ذوات الامثال (dhawat al-amthal): Fungible items that are similar in kind and can be replaced by equivalent measure, weight, or number.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following sales is considered batil due to the absence of the condition that both exchange items be mal?
A) Sale of a car with a deferred price
B) Sale of blood
C) Sale of wheat with an unknown measure
D) Sale of a house with a known price

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, possession transfers ownership only if taken with the seller's permission (implicit in the majlis suffices) and both items are property; the buyer then owes compensation based on the type of goods.

Possession in a Fasid Sale: The Role of Implicit Permission
Ruling with proof from al-Hidayah on possession in fasid sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for possession in a fasid sale according to the Hanafi school and identify when implicit permission suffices and when explicit permission is required.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, buyer's possession before separation is valid by istihsan if seller remains silent — implicit permission from the contract itself.

This lesson examines a key ruling from al-Hidayah regarding possession (qabd) in a voidable (fasid) sale. While the apparent position (zahir al-riwayah) requires explicit permission from the seller, the Hanafi school, by istihsan, accepts implicit permission (dalalat al-ijazah) when the buyer takes possession in the seller's presence before they separate from the contract session. This ruling also extends to gifts, where possession stands in place of acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The buyer's physical possession (qabd) of the sold item in a fasid sale. The ruling is that such possession establishes ownership only if the seller grants permission. Determine the default ruling.
The act is qabd in a fasid sale; the hukm is sahih (valid) under certain conditions.
2
Note the apparent position (zahir al-riwayah)
According to the transmitted texts, explicit permission (amr) from the seller is required for possession to be effective. This is the position of al-Quduri and others.
Zahir al-riwayah: explicit permission needed.
3
Understand the istihsan exception
By istihsan (juristic preference), implicit permission suffices. This occurs when the buyer takes possession in the seller's presence before they separate from the contract session, and the seller does not object. This is the mufta bihi position.
Implicit permission (dalalat al-ijazah) inferred from seller's silence.
4
Trace the proof (wajh al-dalalah)
The proof for istihsan: The seller's entry into the contract implies giving the buyer power to take possession. Therefore, the buyer's possession in the seller's presence without objection is considered as if permitted.
Contractual implication overrides strict requirement of explicit command.
5
Note the scope and limitation
Implicit permission only works before separation. After the parties separate, explicit permission is necessary. This ruling also applies to gifts: possession in the session with the donor's silent consent is valid by istihsan.
Separation ends the implied authority; same principle in hibah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

مشتری مبیع کا مالک نہیں ہوتا اسی طرح بغیر بائع کی اجازت کے قبضہ کرنے سے بھی مالک نہ ہوگا۔ ( بنا یہ ) روایت صحیحہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ بائع کی طرف سے عقد بیع کا ارتکاب کرنا یہ مشتری کو بیع پر قبضہ کرنے کی قدرت دینا ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر مشتری نے مجلس عقد میں بائع کی موجودگی میں میچ پر قبضہ کیا اور بائع نے اس کو منع نہیں کیا تو یہ قبضہ بائع کی طرف سے دی ہوئی قدرت کے نتیجہ میں شمار ہوگا اور جو قبضہ بائع کی طرف سے دیا جاتا ہے اس سے ملک ثابت ہو جاتی ہے اس لئے اس قبضہ سے مشتری کی ملک ثابت ہو جائے گی اور دلالہ اجازت بائع پر اکتفاء کیا جائے گا جدا ہونے سے پہلے کی قید اس لئے لگائی کہ جدا ہونے کے بعد قبضہ کو مفید ملک بتانے کے لئے بائع کی صراحتہ اجازت ضروری ہے جدا ہونے کے بعد دلالۃ اجازت کافی نہ ہوگی۔ یہی حال ہبہ کے اندر شئی موہوب پر قبضہ کرنے کا ہے یعنی اگر مجلس ہبہ میں موہوب لہ نے شئی موہوب پر واہب کی موجودگی میں قبضہ کیا اور واہب نے سکوت اختیار کیا اور موہوب لہ کو قبضہ کرنے سے منع نہیں کیا تو موہوب لہ کا یہ قبضہ استحساناً جائز ہے اور مجلس ہبہ کی قید اس لئے لگائی کہ باب ہبہ میں قبضہ رکن ہے اور موہوب لہ کی ملک ثابت ہونے کے حق میں قبول کے قائم مقام ہے پس جس طرح قبول عقد مجلس پر موقوف ہوتا ہے اسی طرح باب ہبہ میں قبضہ کی قدرت دینا بھی مجلس ہبہ پر موقوف ہوگا۔
It is necessary to give power. So when the buyer takes possession in the presence of the seller before separating, and the seller did not prohibit him, this possession will be a result of prior power. Similarly, in the case of a gift, taking possession in the session of gift without explicit permission is valid by istihsan. Explanation: The author of Hidayah says that Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Quduri mentioned this condition: that the buyer's possession of the sold item in a fasid sale be by the command of the seller. He said: 'And if the buyer takes possession of the sold item in a fasid sale by the command of the seller.' By 'amr' is meant the seller's permission. Making permission a condition is the zahir al-riwayah, but there is this much: if the seller's permission is found implicitly, it is sufficient; that is, in the case of implicit permission, possession becomes effective for ownership. The form of implicit permission is that the buyer takes possession of the sold item in the session of contract before separating, in the presence of the seller, and the seller does not prohibit the buyer; this is permissible by istihsan, and this is the correct narration. And the author of al-Idah said that in a fasid sale, the buyer's possession of the sold item without the seller's permission is like not possessing; just as when he does not possess, the buyer does not become owner of the sold item, similarly, by possessing without the seller's permission, he will not become owner. The proof of the sound narration is that the seller's entering into the sale contract gives the buyer power to take possession of the sale item. So if the buyer takes possession in the session of contract in the seller's presence, and the seller does not prohibit him, this possession is considered as a result of the power given by the seller, and possession given by the seller establishes ownership. Therefore, by this possession, the buyer's ownership is established, and implicit permission of the seller is relied upon. The condition 'before separation' is placed because after separation, for possession to effect ownership, explicit permission of the seller is necessary; implicit permission after separation is not sufficient. The same is the case of taking possession of the gifted item in a gift: if in the session of gift, the donee takes possession of the gifted item in the presence of the donor, and the donor remains silent and does not prohibit the donee from taking possession, then this possession of the donee is permissible by istihsan. The condition of the session of gift is placed because in the chapter of gift, possession is a pillar and stands in place of acceptance regarding the establishment of the donee's ownership. Just as acceptance depends on the session of contract, similarly, giving power to take possession in the chapter of gift depends on the session of gift.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah commentary. It explains the condition of the seller's permission for the buyer's possession in a fasid (voidable) sale. According to zahir al-riwayah, explicit permission is required. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), implicit permission suffices if the buyer takes possession in the seller's presence during the contract session before the parties separate, and the seller does not object. The proof given is that entering into the contract implies giving the buyer power to take possession, so implicit permission is sufficient. After separation, explicit permission is required. This ruling also applies to gifts: in a gift, possession during the session with the donor's silent consent is valid by istihsan, because possession in a gift stands in place of acceptance. The passage distinguishes between explicit and implicit permission and clarifies that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that implicit permission before separation is sufficient.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession of the sold or gifted item.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference overriding qiyas for a more equitable ruling.
دلالۃ اجازت (dalalat al-ijazah): Implicit permission inferred from the seller's conduct (e.g., silence when buyer takes possession).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale, when does implicit permission of the seller suffice for the buyer's possession to establish ownership?
A) At any time, even after separation from the session.
B) Only if the seller explicitly states permission.
C) Before the parties separate from the session of contract.
D) Only if the buyer pays the full price.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the mufta bihi position is that implicit permission from the seller (by his silence) suffices for valid possession before the parties separate; after separation, explicit permission is required.

Condition that Both Counter-Values be Property in Sale
States condition of mal and intra-Hanafi dispute on denying price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between batil and fasid sales when one exchange item is not mal or when the price is denied, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A sale requires both items to be mal; denying the price makes it fasid, not batil.

Imam al-Quduri set a fundamental condition for a valid sale: both the sold item and the price must be property (mal). If either is not considered mal in Shariah—like carrion, blood, a free person, or air—the sale is void (batil). But what if the buyer says, 'I bought this without a price'? The Hanafi school has two narrations: one says batil, another says fasid (voidable) and ownership transfers by possession. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the preferred view, based on the nature of sale as an exchange contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its rationale
Al-Quduri requires that both exchange items be mal. This is because the essence (rukn) of sale is exchange of property for property. Without this, the contract lacks its defining element.
Mal in Hanafi fiqh includes any beneficial, tradeable item.
2
Apply the condition to non-mal items
If the sold item or the price is carrion, blood, a free person, or air (not mal), the sale is batil. There is no transfer of ownership, even after possession.
These items have no legal value in Shariah.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on denial of price
When someone says 'I bought without a price,' one narration holds the sale is batil because the pillar is missing. The preferred narration says it is fasid—the denial is ineffective; it is treated as silence, and an absolute exchange contract implies a price.
The proof: negation of price is invalid, so the contract is as if the price was left unspecified (silence) and thus fasid.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and its consequence
The relied-upon position is that the sale is fasid. Upon possession, the buyer's ownership is established, but the defect remains (e.g., need to determine fair price).
This follows the principle that a contract of exchange inherently demands a price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحب قدوری نے دونوں عوضوں کے مال ہونے کی بھی شرط لگائی ہے چنانچہ فرمایا۔ وفی العقد عوضان کل واحد منهما مال ” یہ شرط اس لئے لگائی ہے تا کہ بیع کا رکن (حقیقت) یعنی مبادلته المال بالمال متحقق ہو جائے۔ پس جن صورتوں میں مردار یا خون یا آزاد آدمی یا ہوا کو ثمن بنایا ہو یا مبیع بنایا ہو تو احد العوضین کے مال نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے بیع باطل ہو جائے گی اور اگر ثمن کی نفی کر کے بیع کی مثلاً کہا کہ یہ چیز میں نے بغیر ثمن کے خریدی تو ایک روایت کے مطابق یہ بیع بھی باطل ہوگی کیونکہ جب ثمن کی نفی کی تو بیع کا رکن یعنی مبادلته المال بالمال معدوم ہو گیا اور جب رکن بیع معدوم ہو گیا تو بیع ہی معدوم اور باطل ہوگئی ہے۔ اور ایک روایت میں ثمن کی نفی کے ساتھ بیع منعقد ہو جاتی ہے دلیل یہ ہے کہ ثمن کی نفی کرنا درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ بیع اس کو کہتے ہیں جو دو عوضین پر مشتمل ہو اور جو دو عوضین پر مشتمل نہ ہو وہ بہار صدقہ کہا اتا ہے پس جب ثمن کی نفی کرنا صحیح نہیں تو ایسا ہو گیا گویا مشتری نے ثمن سے سکوت کیا ہے اور اگر کوئی خرید وفروخت کرے اور ثمن کے ذکر سے سکوت کرے تو بیع فاسد ہو کر منعقد ہو جاتی ہے اور قبضہ سے ملک مشتری ثابت ہو جاتی ہے کیونکہ مطلق عقد معاوضہ کا تقاضہ کرتا ہے پس جب مشتری نے ثمن کے ذکر سے سکوت اختیار کیا تو اس کی غرض قیمت کے عوض خریدنا ہو گا پس گو یا بائع نے قیمت کے عوض اس کے ہاتھ فروخت کیا ہے۔
Sahib Quduri also placed the condition that both exchange items be property. Thus he said: 'And in the contract, two exchange items, each of which is property.' This condition is imposed so that the pillar (essence) of sale, i.e., exchange of property for property, is realized. Therefore, in cases where carrion, blood, a free person, or air is made the price or the sold item, then due to one of the two exchange items not being property, the sale is batil (void). And if one negates the price and makes a sale, for example, he says: 'I bought this item without a price,' then according to one narration, this sale is also batil, because when the price is negated, the pillar of sale, i.e., exchange of property for property, is non-existent, and when the pillar of sale is non-existent, the sale itself is non-existent and batil. And in another narration, the sale is concluded with the negation of the price. The proof is that negating the price is not valid because sale is called what comprises two exchange items, and what does not comprise two exchange items is called something else (like a donation). So when negating the price is not valid, it becomes as if the buyer remained silent about the price. And if someone buys and sells and remains silent about mentioning the price, the sale is concluded as fasid, and by possession the buyer's ownership is established, because an absolute contract of exchange demands a price. So when the buyer remains silent about mentioning the price, his aim is to buy for a price, so it is as if the seller sold to him for a price.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah commentary on a condition set by Imam al-Quduri. It states that for a valid sale (bay'), both the sold item (mabi') and the price (thaman) must be property (mal). This is because the essence (rukn) of sale is the exchange of property for property. If either item is not considered mal in the Shariah (e.g., carrion, blood, a free person, air), the sale is batil (void). The passage then discusses an ikhtilaf regarding a sale where the buyer denies the price (e.g., says 'I bought without a price'). One narration says it is batil because the pillar is missing. Another narration says the sale is concluded as fasid (voidable) because denying the price is not valid; instead, it is treated as silence about the price. Since an absolute exchange contract implies a price, the sale becomes fasid, and possession establishes ownership. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the sale is fasid, not batil.

Key Terms
مال (mal): Property that has value and is tradeable in Shariah; excludes carrion, blood, free persons, air.
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale transaction.
بیع باطل (bay' batil): A void sale that does not transfer ownership even after possession.
بیع فاسد (bay' fasid): A voidable sale that is defective but can transfer ownership upon possession; requires rectification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Batil (void) because the pillar is missing.
B) Fasid (voidable) and possession transfers ownership.
C) Valid (sahih) with an implied price.
D) Null and void with no legal effect.

Key Takeaway
For a valid sale, both items must be mal; if one is not, it's batil. If the price is denied, the sale is fasid, not batil.

Liability Upon Destruction in a Fasid Sale: Qimi vs Mithli
Explains liability upon destruction in a fasid sale: price for qimi, like for mithli.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qimi and mithli goods and state the compensation rule in a fasid sale upon destruction after possession, applying the qiyas to ghasb.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, upon destruction after possession: price for qimi, like for mithli — analogy to usurpation.

When a sale is fasid (invalid in essence) and the buyer takes possession, if the item is destroyed, the buyer must compensate. But the Hanafi school does not give a blanket price: it distinguishes between fungible and non-fungible goods. This passage from al-Hidayah explains the rule and its proof by analogy to ghasb (usurpation).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its object
The ruling is that upon destruction of the sold item after possession in a fasid sale, compensation is wajib. But the type of compensation depends on the nature of the good.
The passage first mentions Quduri's statement that price is due, then qualifies it.
2
Distinguish qimi vs mithli goods
Identify which goods are qimi (non-fungible, e.g., animals) and which are mithli (fungible, e.g., grains, oil). The text uses 'dhawat al-qimat' and 'dhawat al-amthal'.
The distinction is crucial: for qimi, price; for mithli, like.
3
Locate the dalil: qiyas to ghasb
The proof is analogy to usurpation: if a usurper destroys a mithli item, he must return its like; if a qimi item, he pays its price. The same applies here because the buyer's possession in a fasid sale is wrongful (yad 'adiyah) like a usurper's.
The illa (effective cause) is wrongful possession without ownership.
4
Understand the reasoning for preferring like
The like provides greater similarity in form and substance to the destroyed item than price does. Thus, for mithli goods, the like is obligatory to achieve precise restitution.
The exception: if the like becomes impossible, then price is due.
5
Apply the rule to examples
Practice: If a buyer in a fasid sale takes possession of 10 liters of oil (mithli) and it is destroyed, he must return 10 liters of oil. If a cow (qimi) dies, he must pay its market price.
This exercise reinforces the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ قدوری کا قول لزمنہ قیمتہ یعنی بیع فاسد میں مشتری کے مبیع پر قبضہ کرنے کے بعد اگر مبیع ہلاک ہوگئی تو مشتری پر اس کی قیمت واجب ہوگی یہ حکم ذوات القیم میں ہے جیسے جانور عددیات متفاوت لیکن اگر مبیع ذوات الامثال میں سے ہو جیسے مکمیلات موزونات اور مشتری کے پاس ہلاک ہوگئی ہو تو مشتری پر اس کا مثل واجب ہوگا کیونکہ مشتری کے قبضہ کی وجہ سے مبیع مثلی بذات خود مضمون ہوتی ہے یعنی مثلی چیزوں میں بطور ضمان اس کا مثل ہی واجب ہوتا ہے جیسے غصب میں اگر شئی مغضوب غاصب کے پاس ہلاک ہوگئی اور وہ ذوات الامثال میں سے ہے تو غاصب پر اس کا مثل واجب ہوگا اور اگر ذوات القیم میں سے ہو تو اس کی قیمت واجب ہوگی اور ذوات الامثال میں مثل اس لئے واجب ہوتا ہے کہ صورۃ اور معنی مثل میں ہلاک شدہ چیز کے ساتھ برابری زیادہ ہے بہ نسبت مثل معنوی یعنی قیمت کے پس ذوات الامثال میں مثل صوری ہی واجب ہوتا ہے الا یہ کہ مثل صوری ادا کرنے سے عاجز آ جائے۔
The author of Hidayah says that Quduri's statement 'the price is binding on him' means that in a fasid sale, after the buyer takes possession, if the sold item is destroyed, the price becomes obligatory on him. This ruling is for items of value (qimiyyat), such as animals and dissimilar countable things. But if the sold item is of the type of fungible things (mithliyyat), such as weighed or measured items, and it is destroyed in the buyer's possession, then the like (mithl) is obligatory on him, because due to the buyer's possession, the fungible item is itself guaranteed; that is, in fungible things, the like is obligatory as a guarantee, just as in usurpation (ghasb) if the usurped item is destroyed in the usurper's possession and it is of fungible type, the like is obligatory on the usurper, and if it is of value type, its price is obligatory. And in fungible things, the like is obligatory because formal and substantive equality with the destroyed item is greater in the like than in the metaphorical like, i.e., the price. Therefore, in fungible things, the formal like is obligatory unless the formal like becomes impossible to render.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah commentary. It explains the liability of the buyer in a fasid sale after possession. If the sold item is destroyed, the buyer must compensate. The rule differentiates between two categories: qimi (non-fungible) items, such as animals, where the market price is owed; and mithli (fungible) items, such as goods sold by weight or measure, where the buyer must return an identical item (mithl). The proof is analogy (qiyas) to ghasb (usurpation): if a usurper destroys a mithli item, he must return its like; if a qimi, he pays its price. The reason is that the like provides greater similarity in form and substance than price. However, if the like cannot be obtained, then price becomes due. This reflects the Hanafi focus on precise restoration where possible.

Key Terms
قیمی (qimi): Non-fungible property that varies in value with individual units (e.g., animals, antiques).
مثلی (mithli): Fungible property that can be exactly replaced (e.g., grains, oil).
غصب (ghasb): Unlawful usurpation of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The market price.
B) The like (mithl) of the item.
C) Nothing, because the sale was void.
D) Double the value.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, compensation upon destruction after possession depends on whether the good is fungible (mithli) or non-fungible (qimi), following the analogy of usurpation.

Right of Rescission in a Fasid Sale
Ruling on rescission of a fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for rescission of a fasid sale and distinguish between the right of rescission when the defect is in the essence versus when it is in an excessive condition.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, the right to rescind depends on the type of defect: core defects give both parties the right; conditional defects only the beneficiary.

This lesson examines a ruling from Quduri's matn, as commented upon in Al-Hidayah, regarding the right of rescission (faskh) in a fasid (voidable) sale. The passage clarifies that while both contracting parties have the general right to rescind before possession, after possession the right is determined by the nature of the defect—whether it lies in the essence of the contract or in an excessive condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage is about the right to rescind a fasid sale. The ruling is that rescission is permissible (sahih) for the purpose of removing corruption.
The act is rescission, not the original sale.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is based on rational reasoning (talil): the nature of the fasid sale and the strength of its corruption or contract.
No direct textual evidence is cited; the ruling is derived from the legal effect of fasad.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Before possession, the fasid sale does not transfer ownership, so both parties have the right to refrain from becoming owners—thus both can rescind. After possession, the right depends on whether the corruption is in the essence (strong) or in a condition (weak relative to the contract).
The distinction is based on the relative strength of the defect versus the contract.
4
Apply the distinction
If the defect is in the essence (e.g., riba or forbidden subject matter), both parties can rescind. If the defect is in an excessive condition (shart za'id) that benefits one party, only that beneficiary can rescind.
Example of essence: selling one dirham for two dirhams. Example of condition: seller stipulates a loan from the buyer.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
The ruling protects the party disadvantaged by the condition while upholding the contract's strength for the other party. Understanding this prevents the misconception that rescission is always mutual after possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The contracting parties have the right to rescind the fasid sale. He (al-Quduri) said: 'And for each of the two contracting parties, rescission of it to remove the corruption. This before possession is apparent, because it has not given its ruling (effect), so rescission is refraining from its ruling. And similarly after possession, if the corruption is in the essence of the contract, because of the strength of the corruption. And if the corruption is due to an excessive condition, then the one for whom the condition is made has that (right), not the one upon whom it is made, because of the strength of the contract, except that mutual agreement has not been realized regarding the one for whom the condition is made.' Translation: Quduri said that each of the contracting parties has the option to rescind the fasid sale to remove the corruption. This option before possession is apparent, because the fasid sale has not given the ruling of sale; so rescission is refraining from that ruling. And similarly after possession, when the corruption is in the essence of the contract, because the corruption is strong. And if the corruption is in an excessive condition, then the one for whom the condition is made has the option, not the one upon whom it is made, because the contract is strong, except that satisfaction has not been fully realized regarding the one for whom the condition is made. Explanation: The scenario is that each of the contracting parties has the option to rescind the fasid sale to remove the corruption, whether before or after possession. Before possession, the option is because the fasid sale does not give ownership; so rescinding is refraining from becoming owner, and both seller and buyer have the right to refrain from ownership. So both have the right to rescind. After possession, there are two cases: either the corruption is in the essence of the contract, such as selling one dirham for two dirhams, or selling cloth for wine; or the corruption is in an excessive condition, such as the seller stipulates that the buyer give him a loan or gift, or sets an unknown term for payment. If the corruption is in the essence, then...
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah commentary. It addresses the right of rescission (faskh) in a fasid (voidable) sale. According to Imam al-Quduri, each party has the right to rescind to remove the corruption. Before possession, this is straightforward because possession does not transfer ownership. After possession, the ruling depends on the nature of the defect: if the corruption is in the essence of the contract (e.g., riba or prohibited subject matter), both parties retain the right to rescind because the defect is strong. If the corruption is due to an excessive condition (shart za'id) that benefits one party (e.g., a stipulation for a loan), then only the party for whom the condition is made (the beneficiary) can rescind, because the contract is otherwise strong and the other party is bound by his agreement. The commentary explains the rationale: before possession, neither party has ownership, so both can walk away. After possession with a core defect, the contract is too flawed to enforce, so both can cancel. With a conditional defect, the contract is strong except for the stipulation, so only the one disadvantaged by the stipulation can rescind to undo the injustice.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or cancellation of a contract to remove its legal effect.
صلب عقد (sulb al-'aqd): The essence or core of the contract; a defect here makes the contract fundamentally flawed.
شرط زائد (shart za'id): An additional stipulation beyond the basic contract terms that may cause fasad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale after possession, if the defect is due to an excessive condition that benefits the seller (e.g., stipulating a loan), who has the right to rescind?
A) Both seller and buyer.
B) Only the seller (beneficiary).
C) Only the buyer (obligated party).
D) Neither; the sale is final.

Key Takeaway
The right to rescind a fasid sale after possession is not always mutual; it depends on whether the corruption is in the contract's essence (mutual) or in a beneficial condition (only for the beneficiary).

Mithl vs Qimah in Fasid Sales After Destruction
Liability for destroyed goods in fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mithliyyat and qimiyyat and apply the corresponding compensation rule (mithl or qimah) in a fasid sale after destruction.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, fungible goods require exact replacement; non-fungibles require market value.

When a sale is invalid (fasid) but the buyer takes possession and the item is destroyed, the buyer is still liable. However, the type of compensation depends on the nature of the item. Is it a fungible (mithli) that has standard equivalents, or a non-fungible (qimi) unique in value? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling, grounded in qiyas on ghasp.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the destruction of goods after possession in a fasid sale. The ruling is liability (daman) on the buyer. Confirm the hukm level: wajib.
The obligation is a wajib liability, not a fard.
2
Classify the destroyed item
Determine whether the item is mithli (fungible) or qimi (non-fungible). Mithli items have standard units (e.g., wheat, oil); qimi items vary in value (e.g., animals, land).
Hanafi fiqh defines mithliyyat as items that can be measured, weighed, or counted.
3
Apply the compensation rule
For mithliyyat, the buyer must pay an exact like (mithl ṣūrī). For qimiyyat, he pays the market value (qimah).
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Trace the qiyas analogy
The ruling is analogized to ghasp (usurpation): possession without valid ownership creates a guarantee. The same distinction between mithli and qimi applies.
The illa is 'possession without valid ownership creating daman'.
5
Note the exception
If the buyer cannot procure an exact like, he falls back to paying the value. This is an exception for mithliyyat.
This shows the hierarchy: mithl preferred, then qimah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيوع
۲۲۱
اشرف الهدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد هشتم ہے پس جب ثمن کی نفی کرنا صحیح نہیں تو ایسا ہو گیا گویا مشتری نے ثمن سے سکوت کیا ہے اور اگر کوئی خرید وفروخت کرے اور ثمن کے ذکر سے سکوت کرے تو بیع فاسد ہو کر منعقد ہو جاتی ہے اور قبضہ سے ملک مشتری ثابت ہو جاتی ہے کیونکہ مطلق عقد معاوضہ کا تقاضہ کرتا ہے پس جب مشتری نے ثمن کے ذکر سے سکوت اختیار کیا تو اس کی غرض قیمت کے عوض خریدنا ہو گا پس گویا بائع نے قیمت کے عوض اس کے ہاتھ فروخت کیا ہے۔ (کفایہ)
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ قدوری کا قول "لزمنہ قیمتہ یعنی بیع فاسد میں مشتری کے مبیع پر قبضہ کرنے کے بعد اگر مبیع ہلاک ہوگئی تو مشتری پر اس کی قیمت واجب ہوگی یہ حکم ذوات القیم میں ہے جیسے جانور عددیات متفاوت لیکن اگر مبیع ذوات الامثال میں سے ہو جیسے مکمیلات موزونات اور مشتری کے پاس ہلاک ہوگئی ہو تو مشتری پر اس کا مثل واجب ہوگا کیونکہ مشتری کے قبضہ کی وجہ سے مبیع مثلی بذات خود مضمون ہوتی ہے یعنی مثلی چیزوں میں بطور ضمان اس کا مثل ہی واجب ہوتا ہے جیسے غصب میں اگر شئی مغضوب غاصب کے پاس ہلاک ہوگئی اور وہ ذوات الامثال میں سے ہے تو غاصب پر اس کا مثل واجب ہوگا اور اگر ذوات القیم میں سے ہو تو اس کی قیمت واجب ہوگی اور ذوات الامثال میں مثل اس لئے واجب ہوتا ہے کہ صورۃ اور معنی مثل میں ہلاک شدہ چیز کے ساتھ برابری زیادہ ہے بہ نسبت مثل معنوی یعنی قیمت کے پس ذوات الامثال میں مثل صوری ہی واجب ہوتا ہے الا یہ کہ مثل صوری ادا کرنے سے عاجز آ جائے ۔
Book of Sales (Page 221). Ashraf al-Hidayah – Urdu commentary of Hidayah, Volume 8. When denial of price is not correct, it is as if the buyer remained silent about the price. And if someone makes a purchase and sale and remains silent about mentioning the price, the sale is fasid (invalid) but it is concluded, and with possession, ownership of the buyer is established, because an absolute contract requires consideration. So when the buyer remained silent about mentioning the price, his aim is to purchase for a price, so it is as if the seller sold it to him for a price. (Kifayah)
The author of Hidayah says: Al-Quduri's statement 'he owes its value' means: in a fasid sale, after the buyer takes possession of the sold item, if the item perishes, the buyer owes its value. This ruling applies to items of value (dhawat al-qim), like animals, different numerables. But if the sold item is from fungibles (dhawat al-amthal), like measured and weighed items, and it perishes in the buyer's possession, the buyer owes its like (mithl), because due to the buyer's possession, the fungible item itself becomes liable. In fungibles, as a guarantee, its like is obligatory, like in usurpation: if a usurped item perishes with the usurper and it is from fungibles, the usurper owes its like; if from items of value, he owes its value. And in fungibles, the like is obligatory because similarity in form and meaning with the destroyed item is greater than the meaning-like, i.e., value. So in fungibles, the formal like is obligatory, unless he becomes unable to pay the formal like.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the legal consequence when an item sold under a fasid (invalid) contract is destroyed after possession by the buyer. The ruling distinguishes between two categories of property: qimiyyat (non-fungibles, unique items where value varies, e.g., animals) and mithliyyat (fungibles, uniform items that can be replaced by equivalents, e.g., wheat, oil). For qimiyyat, the buyer must pay the market value (qimah) at the time of destruction. For mithliyyat, the buyer must deliver an equivalent (mithl) of the same kind and quality. This ruling stems from the principle that possession of goods under an invalid sale creates liability (daman), analogous to unlawful usurpation (ghasp). The author emphasizes that in fungibles, the primary obligation is to provide an exact equivalent—a principle derived from the idea that formal similarity (ṣūratī) ensures a more just compensation than monetary value. Only if the buyer cannot procure the exact equivalent does he fall back on paying its value.

Key Terms
ذوات القیم (dhāt al-qīm): Items whose value differs widely and have no standard equivalent, like animals or unique artworks.
ذوات الامثال (dhāt al-amthāl): Items that can be measured, weighed, or counted and have uniform equivalents, like wheat or oil.
مثل صوری (mithl ṣūrī): A physical equivalent identical in form and substance to the destroyed item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale, if a sheep (qimiyy) dies after the buyer takes possession, what is the buyer's obligation?
A) Pay an equivalent sheep (mithl)
B) Pay the market value (qimah)
C) Nothing, because the sale is invalid
D) Return the carcass

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, compensation after destruction depends on the nature of the sold item: fungibles require exact replacement (mithl), non-fungibles require market value (qimah).

Right to Rescind a Fasid Sale: Core Defect vs. Condition Defect
Detailed exposition of the right to faskh in fasid sale with ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between a defect in the core of a fasid sale and a defect in an extra condition, and apply the Hanafi rules on who may rescind and when presence is required.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, the right to rescind depends on whether the defect is in the core or in a condition; after possession, only the beneficiary of the condition may rescind according to Imam Muhammad.

When a contract of sale is defective (fasid), the Sharia grants the parties a right to rescind (faskh) to remove the defect. However, the details of this right vary based on the nature and location of the defect. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position and the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf concerning rescission before and after possession, focusing on the distinction between defects in the core of the contract (ṣalb al-ʿaqd) and defects in an extra condition (sharṭ zāʾid).

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi rule on rescission of a fasid sale
Identify that both contracting parties have the right to rescind any fasid sale to eliminate the defect. This is the default position from al-Quduri's matn.
The right exists whether the defect is discovered before or after possession, but the details differ.
2
Distinguish between a defect in the core and a defect in an extra condition
A defect in the core (ṣalb al-ʿaqd) means the counter-values themselves are invalid (e.g., selling one dirham for two, or selling wine). A defect in an extra condition (sharṭ zāʾid) is an additional stipulation that is not part of the essence (e.g., requiring a loan). Recognise that the strength of the defect affects the binding nature of the contract.
Core defects are considered strong (qawī); condition defects are weak, leaving the contract strong.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the requirement of the partner's presence
When rescinding a fasid sale with a core defect, the two (Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad) require the rescinding party to do so in the presence of the other. Abū Yūsuf allows rescission even in absentia. Analyse the proofs: Abū Yūsuf says it is a Sharīʿa right; the two say it involves an accusation of harm.
The text indicates preference for the view of the two regarding presence.
4
Analyse the second ikhtilaf: who may rescind after possession when the defect is in a condition
Imām Muḥammad restricts the right to rescind to the party benefited by the condition (e.g., the seller if he stipulated a loan). The Shaykhayn (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf) grant the right to both parties. Examine the reasoning: Muḥammad argues the contract is strong but consent is lacking for the beneficiary; the Shaykhayn treat the defect as rendering the contract non-binding.
The author of al-Hidāyah only cites Muḥammad's view, suggesting it is the muftā bihī.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) and the mufta bihi
Apply the rules to a concrete scenario: a seller sells cloth with a condition that the buyer give him a loan. After possession and delivery, the buyer fails to give the loan. According to Muḥammad, only the seller can rescind; according to the Shaykhayn, both can. The mufta bihi for condition defects is Muḥammad's view. For presence, the view of the two requiring presence is most likely operative.
This ikhtilaf does not affect the validity of the underlying fasid sale—both agree the sale is fasid.

متعاقدین کو بیع فاسد کے فسخ کرنے کا حق ہے
قال ولكل واحد من المتعاقدين فسخه رفعا للفساد وهذا قبل القبض ظاهر لانه لم يفد حكمه فيكون الفسخ امتناعا منه وكذا بعد القبض اذا كان الفساد فى صلب العقد لقوته وان كان الفساد بشرط زائد فلمن له الشرط ذالك دون من عليه لقوة العقد الا انه لم يتحقق المراضاة في حق من له الشرط ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا متعاقدین میں سے ہر ایک کو فساد دور کرنے کے لئے بیع فاسد کو فسخ کرنے کا اختیار ہے اور یہ اختیار قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے تو ظاہر ہے کیونکہ بیع فاسد نے حکم بیع کا فائدہ نہیں دیا ہے پس فسخ کرنا اس حکم سے رکنا ہوگا اور یوں ہی قبضہ کے بعد بھی جبکہ فساد صلب عقد میں ہو کیونکہ فساد قوی ہے اور اگر فساد شرط زائد میں ہو تو جس کے واسطے شرط ہے اس کو فسخ کا اختیار ہے نہ کہ اس کو جس پر شرط ہے کیونکہ عقد قوی ہے مگر یہ کہ اس شخص کے حق میں رضامندی پوری نہیں ہوئی جس کے لئے شرط ہے۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ فساد دور کرنے کے لئے متعاقدین میں سے ہر ایک کو بیع فاسد فسخ کرنے کا اختیار ہے خواہ قبضہ سے پہلے ہو یا قبضہ کے بعد ہو قبضہ سے پہلے فسخ کر دینے کا اختیار تو اس لئے ہے کہ قبضہ مبیع فاسد مفید ملک نہیں ہوتی پس قبضہ سے پہلے فسخ کرنا مالک ہونے سے رکنا ہوگا اور مالک ہونے سے رکنے کا اختیار بائع اور مشتری دونوں کو ہے یعنی بائع ثمن کا مالک ہونے سے رک سکتا ہے اور مشتری مبیع کا مالک ہونے سے رک سکتا ہے پس جب دونوں کو مالک ہونے سے رکنے کا اختیار ہے تو دونوں کو فسخ کرنے کا بھی اختیار ہے اور قبضہ کے بعد میں دو صورتیں ہیں فساد یا تو صلب عقد میں ہوگا یعنی احد البدلین میں فساد ہوگا جیسے ایک درہم کو دو درہم کے عوض بیچنا یا کپڑے کو شراب کے عوض بیچنا اور یا شرط زائد میں فساد ہوگا جیسے بائع نے سامان فروخت کرتے وقت یہ شرط لگائی کہ مشتری اس کو قرضہ دے یا ہدیہ پیش کرے یا فروخت کیا اور ثمن کی ادائیگی کے لئے میعاد مجہول مقرر کی پس اگر فساد صلب عقد میں ہو تو چونکہ اس صورت میں فساد بہت قوی ہے اور فساد دور کرنا شریعت کا حق ہے اس لئے حق شرع کی وجہ سے عقد فاسد لازم نہ رہا اور عقد غیر لازم کو عاقدین میں سے ہر ایک فسخ کرنے کی قدرت رکھتا ہے اسلئے اس صورت میں عاقدین میں سے ہر ایک کو فسخ کرنے کا اختیار دیا گیا ہاں اتنا فرق ہے کہ طرفین کے نزدیک جو بھی فسخ کرنا چاہے اپنے ساتھی کی موجودگی میں فسخ کرے یعنی اپنے ساتھی کے علم میں لا کر فسخ کرے اور امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک اس کی موجودگی اور عدم موجودگی دونوں حالتوں میں فسخ کر سکتا ہے امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ بیع فاسد کو فسخ کر کے فساد کو دور کرنا شریعت کا حق ہے اور شریعت کا حق ادا کرنے کے لئے کسی کی موجودگی یا عدم موجودگی شرط نہیں ہے اس لئے بیع فاسد کو اپنے ساتھی کی موجودگی میں بھی فسخ کر سکتا ہے اور عدم موجودگی میں بھی فسخ کر سکتا ہے۔
طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ بیع فاسد کو فسخ کر کے فساد دور کرنا باب شریعت کا حق ہے مگر فسخ کرنے میں اپنے ساتھی پر الزام بھی ہے کیونکہ آدمی عقد اس وقت فسخ کرتا ہے جبکہ وہ اس میں ضرر محسوس کرتا ہے گویا فسخ کرنے والے نے اپنے ساتھی پر ضرر پہنچانے کا الزام لگایا ہے اور کسی پر الزام لگانے کے لئے ضروری ہے کہ اس کو معلوم بھی ہو جس پر الزام لگایا ہے۔ اس لئے فسخ کرنے والا اپنے ساتھی کی موجودگی میں فسخ کر سکتا ہے اس کی عدم موجودگی میں فسخ کرنے کی اجازت نہیں ہے اور اگر فساد شرط زائد میں ہو تو قبضہ مشتری سے پہلے عاقدین میں سے ہر ایک کو فسخ کرنے کا اختیار ہے لیکن قبضہ کے بعد حضرت امام محمد کے نزدیک صرف اس کو فسخ کرنے کا اختیار ہے جس کے لئے شرط کا نفع ہو مثلا قرضہ دینے یا ہدیہ دینے کی شرط میں بائع کا نفع ہے اور میعاد مجہول کی شرط میں مشتری کا نفع ہے اور شرط جس کے خلاف ہو اس کو فسخ کرنے کا اختیار نہیں ہے مگر یہ یادر ہے کہ من لہ الشرط اگر اپنے اختیار کے تحت فسخ کرنا چاہے تو من علیہ الشرط کی موجودگی میں فسخ کرے اور شیخین کے نزدیک عاقدین میں سے ہر ایک کو فسخ کرنے کا اختیار ہے صاحب ہدایہ نے فقط امام محمد کا قول ذکر کیا ہے شیخین کا قول ذکر نہیں کیا حضرت امام محمد کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شرط زائد میں فساد پیدا ہونے سے فساد قوی نہیں ہوتا بلکہ عقد قوی ہوتا ہے لیکن اب سوال ہو گا کہ جب عقد قوی ہے تو عاقدین میں سے کسی کو بھی اختیار نہ ہونا چاہیے تھا اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ بغیر فسخ کے چونکہ من لہ الشرط کی رضا مندی متحقق نہیں ہوتی اس لئے اس کو فسخ کرنے کا اختیار دیا گیا اور شیخین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ فساد دور کرنا چونکہ شریعت کا حق ہے اس لئے عقد لازمی نہ رہا یعنی عقد غیر لازم ہو گیا اور عقد غیر لازم کو عاقدین میں سے ہر ایک فسخ کر سکتا ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں عاقدین میں سے ہر ایک کو بیع فاسد کرنے کا اختیار ہوگا۔
The right of each contracting party to rescind the fasid sale.
He (al-Quduri) said: 'And each of the two contracting parties has the right to rescind it, to remove the defect. This is apparent before possession, because it has not brought about its legal effect, so rescission is a cessation from it. Similarly, after possession, when the defect is in the core of the contract, due to its strength. And if the defect is in an extra condition, then the one for whom the condition is made has that right, not the one against whom it is made, due to the strength of the contract, except that consent is not achieved with respect to the one for whom the condition is made.'
Translation: Al-Quduri stated that each of the two contracting parties has the option to rescind the fasid sale to remove the defect. This option is obvious before possession, because the fasid sale has not conferred the effect of sale, so rescinding means refraining from that effect. The same applies after possession when the defect is in the core of the contract (salab al-aqd), because the defect is strong. But if the defect is in an extra condition (shart za'id), then only the party for whom the condition was stipulated has the right to rescind, not the party against whom it was stipulated, because the contract is strong, except that full consent was not realized for the party who has the condition.
Explanation: The issue is that each contracting party has the right to rescind the fasid sale to remove the defect, whether before or after possession. Before possession, the right to rescind exists because the possession in a fasid sale does not confer ownership; thus rescission before possession means refraining from becoming owner. Both seller and buyer can refrain from becoming owner—the seller from becoming owner of the price, the buyer from becoming owner of the sold item. So when both have the right to refrain from ownership, they both have the right to rescind. After possession, there are two scenarios: the defect is either in the core of the contract, i.e., in one of the two counter-values, such as selling one dirham for two dirhams, or selling cloth for wine; or the defect is in an extra condition, such as the seller stipulating that the buyer give him a loan or a gift, or setting an unknown term for payment. If the defect is in the core of the contract, because the defect is very strong and removing the defect is a right of the Sharia, therefore due to the Sharia right, the fasid contract does not remain binding, and a non-binding contract can be rescinded by either party. So in this case, each contracting party is given the right to rescind. However, there is a difference: according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?), whoever wishes to rescind must do so in the presence of his partner, i.e., inform him. But according to Imam Abu Yusuf, he can rescind in both states—presence or absence. Imam Abu Yusuf's proof is that rescinding the fasid sale to remove the defect is a Sharia right, and fulfilling a Sharia right does not require the presence or absence of anyone; therefore, he can rescind both in the presence of his partner and in his absence. The proof of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?) is that although removing the defect is a Sharia right, rescission involves accusing one's partner of harm, because a person rescinds a contract only when he perceives harm in it; it is as if the rescinding party has accused his partner of causing harm. And to accuse someone, it is necessary that the accused be aware. Therefore, the rescinding party can only rescind in his partner's presence, not in his absence. And if the defect is in an extra condition, before possession, each party has the right to rescind, but after possession, according to Imam Muhammad, only the one for whom the condition is beneficial has the right, e.g., in a condition to give a loan or gift, the seller benefits; in an unknown term condition, the buyer benefits. The one against whom the condition is made has no right to rescind. Note: if the one with the condition wishes to rescind, he must do so in the presence of the one against whom the condition is made. According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), each contracting party has the right to rescind. The author of Hidayah only mentioned Imam Muhammad's statement, not that of the two shaykhs. Imam Muhammad's proof is that when the defect arises from an extra condition, the defect is not strong; rather, the contract is strong. But a question arises: if the contract is strong, then neither party should have the right to rescind. The answer is that because without rescission, the consent of the party with the condition is not realized, so he is given the right to rescind. The proof of the two shaykhs is that since removing the defect is a Sharia right, the contract becomes non-binding, and a non-binding contract can be rescinded by either party, so in this case each has the right to rescind the fasid sale.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah delves into the right of rescission (faskh) in a fasid (invalid) sale. It explains that both parties have the right to rescind, but the details vary based on the nature and location of the defect. The text is structured as a commentary on al-Quduri's matn. First, before possession, rescission is straightforward: since the sale hasn't transferred ownership, either party can simply refrain from proceeding. After possession, two scenarios are distinguished: (1) If the defect lies in the core of the contract (e.g., riba or selling a prohibited item), the defect is strong, and the contract is rendered non-binding (ghayr lazim). Consequently, both parties retain the right to rescind at any time. (2) If the defect is in an extra condition (shart za'id), the contract is considered strong and binding, but an exception is made for the party benefited by the condition, as his full consent was never realized. The text then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirement of the other party's presence during rescission. Imam Abu Yusuf holds that since rescission is a Sharia right, it can be executed unilaterally without the other party's presence. The other two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, referred to as 'the two') argue that rescission implies an accusation of harm, which requires the accused to be present and informed. Finally, when the defect is in an extra condition and after possession, Imam Muhammad restricts the right of rescission to the party for whom the condition is beneficial, while the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) grant the right to both parties. The author of Hidayah notes that only Imam Muhammad's view is mentioned, implying a preference.

Key Terms
صلب العقد (ṣalb al-ʿaqd): The core of the contract; refers to the essential elements (the two counter-values) that contain the defect, e.g., selling a dirham for two dirhams.
شرط زائد (sharṭ zāʾid): An extra condition appended to the contract that is not from its essence, e.g., a condition to give a loan.
فسخ (faskh): The act of rescinding or annulling a contract unilaterally or bilaterally.
من له الشرط (man lahu al-sharṭ): The party for whose benefit the condition is stipulated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale where the defect is an extra condition (e.g., seller stipulates that buyer must give a loan), according to Imam Muhammad, who can rescind after possession?
A) Both the seller and the buyer
B) Only the buyer
C) Only the seller (the beneficiary of the condition)
D) Neither party can rescind

Key Takeaway
The right to rescind a fasid sale depends on whether the defect lies in the core or in a condition; after possession, the preferred Hanafi view restricts rescission to the beneficiary of the condition, and requires the rescinding party to act in the presence of the other.
